A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,12< a cheque for
jgl 099 13s 6d. in payment of the cash value of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469.822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 20, 1892.
The Imported Criminal.
Ever since the race of globe-trotters first
commenced to pervade Australia, and to
travel on free pauper passes on the Govern-
ment railways, and to deliver lectures, and
make garbled notes, and then go “home”
and write dreary, muddled books about the
things they hadn’t seen and the facts they
hadn’t heard, an impression has been abroad
that Australia is pre-eminently a land of
crime. With hardly an exception, the family
of globe-trotters has been solid on this point,
right down to the lamented Christie
Murray, which insolvent Munchausen
was, perhaps, the most solid of them all.
The Australian larrikin has been officially
inspected, and his howl and oath have been
analysed and reduced to their component
parts, and held up on the fork of righteous
indignation to be the scorn of a moral world.
The football barracker and the native bruiser
and the “sporting man” have all been dis-
sected, and proved to be prodigiously loud-
mouthed and blatant and obnoxious. The
Australian “drunk” is alleged to be more
numerous than the “drunk” in almost any
other part of the world ; the swindlers and
homicides and brutal-assault men are past
counting, and bigamists, thieves, garrotters,
cattle-stealers, horse-stealers, sheep-stealers,
dog - stealers, forgers, rioters, incendi-
aries, gamblers, loafers, libellers, per-
jurers, embezzlers, counterfeit - coiners,
burglars, bushrangers, and people who aban-
don illegitimate infants on doorsteps all help
to make up a mass of criminality at which
the moral British nation is officially requested
to shudder. And the godly Bull has shud-
dered accordingly. Nearly every literary
visitor who has infested Australia for the last
25 years has gone back to complain of how
we have degenerated from the lofty British
standard, and the mother-land has grieved
in many leading articles accordingly.
# * #
In one respect the literary globe-trotter is
correct. Australia has a very high average
of criminality. There is nothing to be gained
by denying this fact, even if we had any
wish to deny it. The average is far
from being the highest- among civilised
nations, but the exact proportion between
Australian crime and that of Europe and
America is a matter into which we have no
time at present to inquire. The point at
issue is, that what is commonly described as
Australian crime is really imported British
crime ; that the malefactor who arouses a
shudder in the bosom of the moral British
visitor is his own malefactor ; and that the
offensive Englishman who wails in print over
the degradation which prevails in Australia
really supplies the degradation that he wails
over. There is a wondrous hypocrisy about
that Anglo-Saxon pietist who furnishes this
whole continent with drunkkrds and law-
breakers, and then is pre-eminent in
grief over the falling-away of the nation
which he has contaminated with the offal of
his three kingdoms, but it is strictly in
keeping with his entire history. He even
feels hurt that Australia is not sufficiently
grateful for that offal, and is indignant
because the prospect of another large con-
signment, heralded by the dissonant tam-
bourine and the great Salvation drum,
arouses no enthusiasm in the land.
* * *
At the present time, and so far as can be
ascertained from the figures immediately
available, New South Wales is the most con-
spicuously criminal of the Australian pro-
vinces. The population of New South Wales
may be roughly described as consisting of 72
per cent, natives of Australia, about 26 per
cent, imported inhabitants from the British
islands, and the balance—with which we
have, at present, nothing to do—of various
nationalities. The native population is thus
very nearly three times as numerous as that
which has been sent here in a ready-made
condition from Great Britain, aud it is at
least reasonable to suppose that the native-
grown criminal would also be three times as
numerous as the exotic article. He ought,
indeed, to be far more numerous to justify
the lofty moral attitude assumed by the
average British critic, but leaving out the
tttitude of that pompous censor, it seems
fair to assume that the two nations would
prove equally law-abiding and equally law-
less. It is rather surprising, therefore, to
find that, out of over 38,000 criminals, great
and small, arrested in New South Wales
during 1890—the last year for which the
figures are available—only about 15,000 were
Australians, while over 19,000 were natives
of Britain. In other words, the Briton, in
proportion to his numbers, is about four-
times as criminal as the Australian ; and
when this outrageous national malefactor
publishes criticisms upon the law-abiding
person whose land he infests, and the
offender remonstrates—because of his own
offences—with the person who didn’t commit
these offences, the great art of Pecksniffism
reaches its climax.
* * #
In all the many branches of criminality
there is hardly one in which the imported
Briton is not pre-eminent. He supplies
8700 drunkards against 3900 locally-g rown
dipsomaniacs, and is thus, in proportion to
numbers, more than six times as drunk as
the native inebriate. Under the headings,
“murder, attempted murder, and man-
slaughter,” he furnishes 21 offenders against
31 of Australian origin, thereby showing
himself, man for man, twice as murderous
as the rest of the population. In the matter
of assaults, plain and garnished with robbery
and other trimmings, the arrests show 778
of British, against 1149 of Australian birth,
which—again allowing for the relative num-
bers of the two nations—shows that the
Briton is just twice as much addicted to
leaping on an inoffensive stranger with his
hobnailed boots as is the humble aboriginal
rowdy of the south. And, strangely enough,
it is under the designation of “aggravated
assault ” that the Englishman is most con-
spicuous ; he loves to prance on the head of
his prostrate victim and gouge his eye out or
kick his teeth down his throat, and at the
same time he complains sadly in the Fokt-
niohtly Review that the Australian has “ a
strain of cruelty ”in his composition. In
every variety of crime which implies revolt-
ing brutality the imported person keeps far
ahead. Out of seven garrotters arrested,
four were English, which is exactly four
times their fair proportion. Under the
headings of “wounding” and “inflicting
grievous bodily harm ” the cultured Briton
is nearly as prominent. He comes in well
in front, too, in the matter of “cruelty to
animals,” which is a strictly British charac-
teristic, and in the matter of “wife and
child desertion,” which may possibly be
placed under the same category, he is three
times as conspicuous as the native-born
inhabitant.
# # •
The Australian, it is commonly asserted,
is a diseased person with a tendency to
erotic mania, and many frantic newspapers
with a longing for unclean sensationalism
have bewailed this alleged tendency to all
manner of sexual offences. The assertion,
according to the indisputable evidence of
figures is a lie—the same dreary lie as all the
rest. In those dismal crimes that are
described in the records as “rape” and
“ indecent assault ” the British section of the
population is, as usual, in a front seat. They
furnish, in proportion to numbers, about
twice as many offenders as do the native
Australians. They also supply five times as
many lunatics, six times as many cases of
indecency, three times as many of riotous
and disorderly conduct, and five times as
many of vagrancy, as do the rest of the
population. In every sort and description
of stealing—larceny, cattle, horse, and sheep-
stealing ; embezzlement, forgery, false pre-
tences, arson, coining, and all kinds of plain
and fancy felony—the British leprosy is well
to the front, and the British criminal is 50
per cent, more numerous than the one of
local manufacture. He also keeps ahead in
all the small offences which lend a light and
humorous aspect to the solemn poliorecords.
He refuses to pay his cab-fare, and thereby
defrauds a hard-working cabman and robs
an exhausted horse of its oats, four times as
often as anybody else in New South Wales,
He is three times as much given to defama-
tory libel as all the rest of the population,
nine times as much given to “illegally
pawning,” and nine times as much addicted
to being a suicided corpse in a river and thus
damaging the water supply. He has the
heading “conspiracy ” all to himself ; he is
in evidence for “ polluting water he de-
clines to pay his train-fare twenty-four times
as often as the Australian native ; he is iu
an enormous majority iu the matter of
hawking without a licence ; he travels
on the railways without a ticket more
than twice as much as any other citi-
zen, and he is more than twice as
much addicted to dog-stealing, contempt of
court, and absconding from bail. Except in
such matters as “ disturbing a congregation ”
and a Very few more, the Englishman is
everywhere predominant. If that imported
Briton could be driven unanimously into a
deep part of the sea, New fcouth Wales
would become —through his loss - one of the
most law-abiding countries upon this earth.
* * #
The British malefactor infests all the Aus-
tralasian provinces to a greater or less
degree; in the majority he is even mote
obnoxious than he is iu New South Wales.
In Maorilaud, in paiticular, the English
population furnishes from ten to thirteen
times more criminals in proportion to num-
bers than do the native-born inhabitants—
the proportion varies, hut for 14 years the
English criminal has never been less than
1000 per cent, in advance of all others. And
unhappily, in all the Australasian provinces,
this obnoxious variety of Anglo-Saxon keeps
steadily increasing. The beetle-browed
British ruffian - the individual with the
bullet-head and the hair like traol doormats
—the sneak-thief and the brutal-assault man
and the embezzler—are for ever coming,
and as they come the pious globe-trotter
keeps on lamenting about the criminality
of this degenerate nation. The English-
man still recognises upon these shores
the ancient sign, “Rubbish may be shot
here.” He sends the rubbish, and reproaches
Australia with having it when it is sent, and
he also suggests military coercion and blood-
shed when this country objects to him send-
ing any more. He is the original offender
and also the individual who wears an air of
solemn reproach concerning the offences.
He pervades every stratum of Australian
society. He supplies this country with
Governors, archbishops, bishops, politicians,
railway officials, and college professors, and
grabs every good salary that is going ; he
also supplies burglars, sneak theives, gar-
rotters, and hoodlums who, being unprovided
with an official salary, get in by the window
at night and grab the family spoons. And,
in addition to all these things, he furnishes
much solemn warning about the necessity of
maintaining a high standard of morality in
“ the kawlinies,” and last of all he wants to
know why we are not grateful for the bene-
fits accruing from the British connection.
Somehow we are not grateful. The bloated
English rowdy who drinks and howls
throughout the continent does not appeal to
the Australian soul as a spectacle that is
calculated to arouse gratitude.
Woman’s Work in Sydney.
It is frequently and loudly asserted that Aus-
tralia is not as other portions of the earth are.
Here we have no proletariat, no poverty, no
caste system —in short, none of the evils or
grievances under which the Old World labours
and groans. This is a land flowing with milk
and honey, teeming with wealth, stuffed with
riches, and exuding fat and plenty. It is a
land of corn and wine and fruit, which God has
kept pure and unspotted throughout the ages
as a guerdon for the Anglo-Saxon race. This
is a beautiful sermon, preached from a lying
text, with which to tickle the cars of Old
World emigrants and solace those self-deluding
Australians whose breath of life is “ blow,” and
whose existence is one eternal brag. As a
matter of fact, we are doing all we can to
exhaust our advantages, and to reduce onr
country to the same level of commercial and
industrial degradation as that reached by
Europe and America. We have imported a
ready-made system which has only to work
sufficiently long to grind ns all to powder—-at
least excepting the few who may manage to
get a grasp on the handle of the driving-wheel.
» # *
The modern industrial system is marked by
a total disregard of the comfort, the con-
venience, the health, nay, the very life of the
raw material of flesh and* blood upon which it
operates for the purpose of extracting profit.
The extreme callousness of the old world
employer may not yet characterise the profit-
monger of Australia, nevertheless the system
has been introduced, it is thriving iu a fat soil,
and wants little encouragement to culminate in
a state of things such as that revealed by the
match-girls’ strike in London. If anyone
wants reliable evidence of the condition of
female-labour in so young a city as Sydney, for
instance, we would refer them to the report on
woman’s work recently prepared by Mr. T. A.
Coghlan, the N.S.W. Government Statistician,
in which all the information obtainable through
the operation of the Census and Industrial
Returns Act of 1891, is classified and tabulated
in a thorough and scientific fashion, forming
a body of well-arranged statistics, some of
which are appalling, when one comes to read
between the lines. In his report, Mr. Coghlan
furnishes the public with data respecting the
tailoring, dressmaking, millinery and white-
work industries, the woollen-cloth manufacture,
and the laundry business.
* * *
The information supplied in this report com-
prises facts concerning accommodation, division
of labour, classification of work, rates of ))a
raent, earnings, hours of labour, fines, earnin'
of home-workers and outside workers ; SVVe J s
ing, employment of children, morality, wa»esai 1
piecework, apprenticeship, overtime, liealth
etc., and gives a very detailed statement j’
reference to the whole field represented by 0"
sewdng and washing trades, and the
cloth manufacture. In regard to accomnioda
tion, one of the most striking features of th"
report is the poor character of the sanitary
arrangements provided for employes. The/
arrangements are seldonl good, very o fter*
inferior, and too frequently had. At one place'
where live men and twenty women are working
there is hut one small closet for general use'
At another place, the sanitary conveniences are
in connection with a large arcade, where the
cabinets for both sexes adjoin, and are separated
only by a corrugated iron fence, six feet i u
height and open at the top. In some cases no
sanitary arrangements exist at all, and this
remark applies to one of the most pretentious
establishments in Sydney —although the fact is
not mentioned in the report. In one of the
suburbs, the nearest conveniences of a sanitary
character to a large tailoring factory are iu
connection with the railway station ! Both
in the large factories and in workshops, where
tailoring is carried on, no attempt is made to
afford facilities for the prevention, extinction or
escape from lire. The approaches to many
workshops are dark and dingy ; the stairways
are rickety, steep and narrow, and lighted by
gas during the daytime. In case of fire they
are practically useless. In the busy season 80
girls and men work in one long room, the
approach to which is a ladder through a trap-
hole. The entire length of the room on the
next floor has to be traversed before a corres-
ponding trap-hole and ladder can be reached.
The tailoress makes more money than any
other employe in the sewing trades, but then
her calling is not so respectable. The home-
worker, however, has rather a bad time of it.
One woman with a family of four to support
was compelled to work twelve and fourteen
hours a day in addition to the time devoted to
her household affairs, and this for an average
remuneration of fourpence half-penny per hour.
The earnings of by far the greater number of
home-working tailoresses range from ten to
fifteen shillings a week. Mr. Coghlan re-
marks :
The tendency to a minimum wage already resulting
from the existence of this large body of incompetent
workers, is still further aggravated by the competition of
women not wholly dependent upon their own earnings.
For example, there are numerous cases where married
women whose husbands earn fair wages seek to increase
their incomes by tailoring work, and it is by this class
that the minimum price for piecework is most frequently
accepted.
We have beard of one instance of a trousers-
hand who was compelled to work hours a
day—los hours a week—to make four pairs of
trousers for which she received from one shil-
ling to two shillings per pair. She had to
furnish her own thread and lining, and her
average earnings were five and sixpence a day
when in full work. Sometimes, however, she
had no more than three pairs per week to make
and her average weekly earnings were twenty
shillings, out of which she had to pay about
sixteen for rent and living. Her clothing was
untidy and slipshod, her person dirty, her room
in a state of woeful disorder—but then she was
at the time working 17i hours per diem, and
had little leisure for what she doubtless con-
sidered superfluous adornment.
* * *
The dressmaker is a grade higher iu the
social scale than the tailoress ; but she makes a
great deal less, money, and she is compelled to
work for many hours longer. Her work-room
is generally over-crowded and badly-ventilated
—very often badly-lighted as well. She is
frequently obliged to toil on without proper
intervals of rest long hours beyond the time for
which she is supposed to be paid. In such a
place she sometimes eats her lunch without
leaving her seat, and then immediately resumes
her work, hurrying on in order to be earlier
released, for she knows from bitter experi-
ence that when a job cannot be completed
within the regular working day, but may
possibly be completed before midnight, 7, S,
9, or even I 1 o’clock, may find her still
frantically plying her needle. The Government
Statistician laments the possible effect on the
morals of young girls of compelling them to
traverse without protection the midnight streets
of a large city, and the injury inflicted on their
bodily health, as very obvious evils :
After bending for ten or twelve hours over their sewing,
girls are too weary to have much appetite for their food,
and on the morrow they rise unrefreshed to face thesanie
monotonous routine of excessive toil, with insufficient
food, rest, and exercise. Saturday is frequently the most
trying day of all, because, expecting to be free at one
o’clock, they take no lunch with them, and on such
occasions it is an ordinary occurrence that they pass y’ e
whole day, from the morning till the evening meal, with-
out refreshment of any kind. It is therefore not to he
wondered at that few of the girls are robust, or that
many exhibit signs of delicate health. . . . In lafff®
establishments the regular hours are sometimes observed
in one room and not in another, because the power to
keep the girls overtime rests largely in the hands of the
forewoman of each room, who is expected to obtain a
fixed result on the work done. The one-sidedness toe
contract between employers and employed in this in-
dustry is well exemplified in certain establishments,
w here the girls are fined half-a-day’s pay if they are ten
minutes late in the morning, and fined again on in®
flimsiest pretext, but, on the other hand, are compelieu
to work overtime without pay, and also in slack fi me Lu e
accept compulsory holidays on the same terms.
greatest sufferers iu this direction are those entire*!
dependent on their earnings for subsistence, the exigei j
cies of whose position silence their protests and conip
them to submit to what they know to be injustice an
oppression. . . Payment for overtime is seldom offer
even in cases where extra hours are most common,
employers claiming that, since the workers are som ■
times absent and no deduction from their pay is made t
public holidays, they may reasonably be expected to
extra work when it is required.
From the foregoing it may readily be seen tha
the dressmaker’s life is anything but a happy
one. As an apprentice, she usually serve 8
without pay for the first half-year, and receives
2s. 6d. a week for the second, finishing * iel
term of apprenticeship (generally two years) on
a remuneration of ss. a week. In large retai
establishments the average wage for skn >
bodice, and sleeve hands is 17s. 3d. ; in dress
makers’ shops, 15s. ; in small
retail shops, 13s. Bd. per week Assistants
home-workers earn an average of 9s. 7d.» an 1
the advantage the home-worker derives tr° n
employing assistants is equal to 15s. lln- P *


. nver giving a profit to the employer o
f 1 i ? o’er week tor each assistant employed

Mtatistician remarks :

through three or four hours of overtime m that state.
The girls are said to be, generally speaking,
heroically moral. The report is especially, and
• tly severe on those who insert in the daily
uaners lying advertisements for numbers of
assistants of various grades although they
really want none, and this simply for the pur-
pose of conveying a false impression of the
magnitude of their business.
# * *
The milliner is socially higher than the dress-
maker, and again, of course, makes less money.
But this is not so bad as it at first appears,
because most girls who engage in this business
have comfortable homes, and mainly desire to
learn how to trim their own hats. The lowest
deep of all is reached by the “ white work
slave, the woman who has served no regular
apprenticeship, who has, as a rule, seen heltei
days,” and who desires to conceal the, fact
that she works for a living. these poor
creatures hardly ever know the names of the
(inns for whom they slave. The work is
delivered to them by a man who drives round
in a buggy, and who pushes one bundle round
the door and takes another away with him.
His thrall may have all the responsibilities of
supporting a family, and is, in addition to the
twelve or thirteen hours devoted to the service
of the needle, is obliged to do the entire work of
the household. One poor creature wrought for
six days, twelve hours a day, to make a dozen
children’s pinafores. For this she received the
sum of 2s. 3d. Theaveragenetweekly wage of the
home-worker is 11s. 9d., and she usually starves
at the rate of Bd. per diem, on slops, which she
calls tea, and bread with a scrape of butter on
it. She eats at her work—if it can be called
eating—and her digestive apparatus becomes so
depraved that meat would be a luxury her
weak stomach could not possibly retain. She
is diaphanous, dreamy, transparent. The
power of lifting has long left her. No muscular
exertion is possible, and the blood has dried up
in her veins. She does not live —she lingers.
A factory girl on white-work may earn 10s. a
week; seldom more than 15s. Excluding
cutters and forewomen, the average wages of
the employes in the shirtmaking industry
amount to 11s. 4d. per week. Homeworkers
employed in the manufacture of Crimean and
woollen shirts and flannel vests, on piecework
for white-work factories, average 11s. fid. a
■ week, or less than per hour. The highest
recorded weekly earning was 15s. for 60 hours,
and the lowest Bs. for 40 hours. The laundress
earns good wages, but it takes a strong woman
to do the work. The domestic servant is better
off than any woman who plies a needle, but
there are a variety of reasons why every woman
who earns her living does not do so by domestic
service. At all events, sufficient has been said
to show that we, in Australia, are rapidly
approaching a condition of things paralleled
only in cities like London and New York —we
have imported their industrial system —and
now we shall sit down and wait for it to grind
out its own damnation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Saving's Bank.
ijAST week a run started upon the Savings name
of New South Wales—almost the only sound
institution of its kind in the province—and for
two days a howling mob raged round the doors
of the office, and climbed frantically over each
other’s heads to get their money or die in the
attempt. It was a wonderful scene while it
lasted, and wonderfully illustrative of the hope-
less, incurable ignorance of the average
depositor— of the man who is to be given by
the ‘"National” Association a vote for his
‘‘ thrift.” The New South Wales Savings Bank
is so affluent that it recently lent the Govern-
ment £600,000 in hard cash ; it is managed by
people unconnected with Sunday-schools; it has
about £1,500,000 on deposit in other banks, and
the leading financial institutions backed their
faith in it by undertaking to casli Savings Bank
deposits to any extent over their own counters.
The Premier announced that t he resources of
the public Treasury were at the bank’s disposal
if they were wanted. A huge notice on the
door of the suspected office set forth, that
it would keep open for three hours beyond the
customary time of closing, and would pay
everybody in gold ; and yet the crowd rushed
in with never a break till at 10.15 p.m. the door
was shut for the night, and then those who
remained still unpaid sat down to weep tears of
despair in the gutter. All through the day
the police were busy rescuing trodden-on
depositors from beneath the feet of the
frenzied crowd and saving fat women in terra-
cotta plush who had got impaled on
the railings in their efforts to climb over.
AH through the night, too, they hovered
nbout, for the depositors sat around on the
edge of the pavement till long after midnight,
nr stood staring aimlessly at the windows,
home who succeeded during the day in drawing
put the savings of a life-time came out rejoic-
mg—and had their pockets picked on the pave-
ment. A lot more rescued their money and
came out saying, ‘Thank God!” —and no
doubt, straightway, in the fullness of their
hearts, went and deposited the assets with
801110 “land bank,” headed by a deacon
renowned for his piety. A lot more, being
unable to ght in, sold their deposits at a huge
discount to some speculative Hebrew who was
cunning a,little three-ball industry of bis own
iu the gutter. Rut the most melancholy
feature of it all was the fact that the
managers of at least one Labour union were
among the earliest to withdraw their account.
Presumably, because they are managers, they
are a shade more intelligent than the mere
rank-and-file of the Union, and if the leaders of
such an organisation know so very little, the
ignorance of the followers must be something
pitiable to contemplate. The whole proceeding
is a miserable commentary on the proposal to
start a Labour Bank, managed by, and for the
exclusive benefit of, the working - classes.
The New South Wales Savings Bank
is, practically, a Labour Bank, with the
additional security of enormous support from
other classes of the community and from the
Government, and the labouring classes did
their level best to pull it down at the first
breath of unfounded suspicion. If it had been
exclusively their own institution they would
have succeeded. No bank can stand being
rushed by all its customers at once, and judging
by last week’s experience that is just what might
readily happen to the Labour Bank in a very
early stage of its career. Moreover, the event
suggests that the public doesn’t like accurate
balance-sheets. The New South Wales Savings
Bank proves, by its balance, that it can pay
225. in the £ ; if it had been an utterly rotten
and fraudulent establishment, it would pro-
bably have issued a document showing that it
had a profit balance of £1,000,000, and was in a
position to pay 30 per cent, on deposits, and
with that obvious lie before them the depositors
would have been quite content. A hook-nosed
adventurer who promises 30 per cent, always
appeals to the finer feelings of the nation more
than a reliable institution which pays four,
declines to receive more than five and a half,
and steadfastly refuses to lend a red cent on
wooden house's.
“General Confidence,”
The Victorian “National Association” of big
money-grubbers has resolved to appeal to the
sympathies of the Australian toiler in a series
of pamphlets, carefully written down to the
presumed level of his intelligence. It calls
these productions “ Plain Facts for Workers,”
probably because they are not plain, neither
are they facts. The first of the series is now
before us : it is a strange collection of fusty
platitudes, and old, blue-moulded aphorisms,
and dried statements, and it is entitled “ Causes
of Prosperity.” The writer begins by slamming
down this problem :
“ Now, what is the corner-stone of public prosperity ?”
Then he answers his own question in the old,
memorable lie that has boomed along for so
many ages:
Every reader who looks beyond his nose will say at once:
“ General confidence !” For what does that imply? A
willingness on the part of capitalists to lay out their
money in enterprises that promise a profitable rate of
interest. And what follows from such an unlocking of
money ? Employment! regular and steady employment
at high rates of remuneration ; and is not this precisely
what the worker wants? Whether he uses his hands or
his brains, are not “work and wages ” his main object
and reward?
In other words, “general confidence” is the
one thing that keeps the world moving. When
everybody has confidence then things are
universally prosperous. The Labour-party, all
over Australia, is seeking to destroy “con-
fidence.” Everybody who wants a rise of wages
is destroying “ confidence.” So are the people
who want to abolish plural voting—in fact, con-
fidence is the one thing needful, and if any single
monopoly is interfered with, this great essential
at once goes to pot. And yet somehow, when
the world has confidence—which happens now
and then, in spasms —it “ gets left ” worse than
when it hasn’t it. The alleged “ corner-stone
of public prosperity ” is the most ruinous thing
that ever happens to the working classes. It
was when they had confidence that they lent
their money to Matthias Larkin, and that
saint swallowed it up. It was public confidence
that led them to take shares in the Premier
Permanent, and the Northumberland Banking
Co., and to leave their deposits with Messrs.
Bell and Davis, and to plunge heavily in the
Land Credit Bank, and to buy hopeless allot-
ments with bad titles from all manner of
Sunday-school superintendents, now in gaol.
“ General confidence ” isn’t a willingness on the
part of capitalists to lay out their money ;” it
is an opportunity for capitalists to scoop in
other people’s money. It lasts just as long as
the masses will lend their money to the capita-
list ; when they stop lending the confidence
game bursts. It always bursts, in fact. Then
when the great boom has gone wrong, and the
poor people who had confidence are cleaned
out, and the people they confided in have Hed
with the proceeds, there is an era of depres-
sion, and the “National Association” says
that it is owing to want of confidence,
arising from wicked agitators, and “ One-Man-
One- Vote” Bills, and similar nefarious proceed-
ings. Vet it would be a vast amount better
for the nation if it never had “confidence.”
There is an almost superhuman gall in the
plaint of the Association about the want of
public faith, seeing that the faith is invariably
misplaced. If the “National Association”
can show one case in all commercial history
when any nation has had what it defines as
“ general confidence,” and hasn’t been hideously
swindled as a consquence, we would be glad to
hear of it.

The Customs v. the Country.
The Melbourne Customs authorities, stimulated
to activity by a steady decline in their “ col-
lections,” have lately been raiding the premises
of sundry manufacturers and merchants con-
cerned in what is practically an advancement
of the protective policy of Victoria. A seizure
of “ German ” lager beer brewed at Collingwood
was the first feat accomplished by the Customs
in this new direction. Secondly, the vigilant
enforcers of the Trade Marks Act descended
upon the Denton Mills Hat Company and
annexed a few gross of “ tiles ” that purported
to be of best English make. In each instance
the parties involved have been guilty of
attempting to mislead the public as to the
birthplace of certain goods. A similar infringe-
ment of the Act can no doubt be proved against
local manufacturers and vendors of boots, t ies,
and sundry other necessaries. Hut as the
public, taking only a superficial view of these
seizures, are apt to think them directed by a
stern moral regard for the commonwealth,
it is just as well to point out that the
Customs people play a purely selfish
hand. Strictly speaking, their object in
seizing sham “imported” articles is to in-
crease the bona-fide foreign trade which pro-
duces the revenue of their particular depart-
ment. In the case of hats, boots, &c., there is no
question as to the action of the Customs being
inspired by Government official rather than by
public interests. Colonial felt, shellac, soles and
“ uppers ” are not injurious to the health, and
even though they be made factors in a deception
practised upon that section of the community
which prefers Hinglish goods for the sake of the
Hinglisli name, the moral fraud is not one that
any patriotic Australian will object to. The
Anglomaniac must be catered for. If he accepts
hats made in Melbourne as the product of his
beloved mother country, so much the better for
Protection. If, on the other hand, the guileless
creature who loves to think that his headgear
comes from London is to have the Government
keeping a vigilant eye open to see that he gets
it—so much the worse for the local industry.
Lager beer, which compares favourably with
the German original in taste, and is equally un-
objectionable, may surely be sold as such
to the benefit of the makers, the unsuspect-
ing consumers, and the country at large. Let
the Customs seizers confine their atten-
tion to the exorcising of evil spirits and
encourage the manufacture of pure Austra-
lian whisky until it will no longer be
necessary for a man who considers his health
to pay duty on the imported lotion, which
is at present so lamentably superior. Bogus
British hats don’t injure a man’s head, but
imported whisky adulterated with Australian
poison has the effect of making it swell badly.
There’s where the Customs ought to come in for
the benefit of the public —and don’t.
Snobbery Organises.
A delirium of sectional organisation has seized
hold of Australian communities. Therehave been
class-organisations, cross-organisations, inter-
organisations, dovetail-organisations, obverse-
cam-reverse organisations, and every other sort
of organisations. The latest is a Melbourne
spume that frothed itself into visibility at a
meeting “to form an association of an anti-
socialistic character amongst young business
men.” There is a fine humour about this phrase
which calls to mind the late Marcus Clarke’s
essay “ On Business Men,” who, he says, “are
the cream of the social bowl—in their own
estimation.”—
You may be the best fellow in the world, the sole sup-
port of an aged mother, and the protector of a whole
boarding-schoolfull of orphan sisters. You may work
like a horse, and give all your goods to feed the poor, but
if you are not a Business Man, you are sounding brass, or
a tinkling cymbal. To be a Business Man is a special
gift—a sort of inherent virtue, like a cast in the eye. If
you are a Business Man, you will succeed in Business —
that is to say, you will be a good husband, a fond father,
a conscientious Christian, always vote with the Govern-
ment, and, when you die, you will go straight to heaven.
If you are a Business Man, people will shout for you, the
papers will write you up, and your friends will give you
prayer-books with gilt-edges.
The Victorian “young business men” have
combined, as Mr. Meudell (son of a banking-
magnate) intimated, because “the black-coat
section of the community suffered as much as
the trades.” It seems supererogatory to organise
snobbery, for snobs always stick together under
any set of circumstances. The same sort of
bastard gentility causes counter-jumpers, and
store-clerks, and calf-padded flunkeys, and
barbers’ mates, and the hair-oiled sections
generally, to roll up whenever corpulent em-
ployers sound the tocsin against men in mole-
skins. The meeting was rather spoilt by a
motion for the formation of “ a political asso-
ciation of young Victorian citizens.” How
“young” must a member be? How much
“Victorian ” ? How much a “ citizen ” ? These
conundrums require a special black-coat solu-
tion, since the black-coat glossary is incompre-
hensible to the ordinary artisan whose horny-
handed, moleskin-lined wages are, in most
cases, much greater than the pittance of the
servile, yet vain, black-coated quill-driver, or
tape-measurer. E. Jowett’s subsequent reso-
lution, however, defined the position clearly—
“ Determined resistance is to be offered to the
proposals of the leaders of the so-called Labour-
party.” It matters not what those proposals
may be —even though they were for the emanci-
pation of the helpless, hopeless, black-coated
brigades themselves —whatever emanates from
the man who soils his hands with manual labour
is “ pizen ” to the black-coated snob, whose
miserable servitude only affords him the hollow
satisfaction that he is expected to possess
neither brains nor grit, nor required to begrime
his cuffs. Let us whoop for organised snobbery.
There is one w T ay, and only one, to restore
confidence in the financial institutions of Aus-
tralia. Let a detective—or, if needful, a staff
of detectives —be attached to each one, and let
him search every director, manager, clerk or
auditor every time he goes out, and if he finds
upon him any cash or documents which might
possibly belong to the bank, let him confiscate
them and deposit them with the Government,
pending an enquiry. If necessary, he should be
authorised to undress the director or other officer
and inspect him by means of a microscope, and
also to feel for documents in his ears and in the
lining of his clothes. There must be only one
exit from the office —through the detective’s
room ; the windows must be barred ; and any
director or manager seen escaping over the roof
must be made liable to be shot at there and
then. No customer must be allowed to com-
municate with an officer of the bank except in
the presence of a detective, and, if neces-
sary, the latter must be authorised to
search the customer also. The office keys
must be kept by the police. The detective
who looks after things must also be searched
six times a day by a Government auditor, and
the Government auditor should be inspected at
frequent intervals by the Premier. All parcels
going in and out of the bank must be examined
and the officers’ lunch overhauled by the aid of
a magnifying glass. Anybody throwing any-
thing out of tiie bank window should be imme-
diately arrested on suspicion of flinging 1 lie
reserve-fund to Ins pal outside. The ash-heap
at the back must also be sifted hourly by the
police, lest notes or other securities should be
planted there and surreptitiously removed at
night by a confederate with a dray. No
manager, clerk, director, or other officer to
leave the town without finding security for his
return, and the charwoman and the coal man
must furnish bonds for their good behaviour.
If this scheme won’t produce any good results,
then the case is about hopeless.
Premier Salisbury states, as a proof of the
impossibility of Home Rule, that the majority
of the Irish people “ have always been allied to
the enemies of England.” The average sample
Bull is so miserably inflated that it never
dawns on him that the sentence might as well
read the other way about —“ the majority of
the English people have invariably been allied
to the enemies of Ireland.” Moreover, the
statement is a lie in any case, for Ireland has
done as much service in fighting the battles of
the Empire as England herself. Right up to
the time of the Crimean War Ireland supplied
four times as many troops, in proportion to
population, as England did, and Ireland, Scot-
land, and Wales furnished among them about
two-thirds of what was termed—with cheerful
mendacity—the English army.. Even away
back in the Middle Ages there was a great Irish
contingent at Cressy, and the remainder of the
alleged English army on that fateful occasion
was mostly Normans and Gascons. An Irish
army was imported to prop up the English
Crown in the Wars of the Roses, when England
herself wouldn’t fight worth a cent. There
never was a British Empire until the time when
Irish and Scotch soldiers were pressed into the
English army ; England, while travelling on her
own account, inherited an empire on the Con-
tinent of Europe, and had neither the strength
nor the brains to keep it, and she afterwards
started at least four foreign empires and lost
them all. Salisbury ought to read up the
history of his country over again. It was a
remarkably poor, mean country till it got the
loan of Ireland, Scotland, and Wales to fight
its battles and prop it up generally. And now
that the loan is being withdrawn it is likely to
be a very poor, mean country again. The Irish
contingent was once nearly 80,000. Twenty -
five years ago it was 50,000. Now it is 28,000.
The Scotch forces are evaporating in the same
way because the Highlands are depopulated by
landlordism, and the Highlanders used to be
the flower of the “ English” army. The Scotch
and Irish troops both began to diminish rapidly
after the Crimea, and since the Crimea Britain
hasn’t raised enough military glory to surprise
its grandmother with.
"O’ "
The Age sneers at the Labour Party in the
coming Victorian elections as people “about
whom we hear very much, and of whom', when
it is all over, we are likely to hear very little.
But the same journal derided the chances of
Trenwith and Hancock before they were re-
turned to Parliament, and many better-liked and
trusted labour candidates than the aforesaid
pair will be going to the poll in April. Maybe,
too, they will pull through without the assist-
ance of an “ organ.” Everybody is beginning
to understand that the Melbourne daily press
represents only rotten “interests, and is
practically the voice of the loyal land-boomer,
exhorting the country to keep its paths crooked.
“ I never knew a candidate to be carried in by
the Age,” says one of Melbourne’s oldest poli-
ticians, “ although he is often pushed in by the
opposition of the Argus.” Now the animosity
of both journals at once should give a great
moral impetus to the enemy.
Melbourne Age has gone back on democratic
liberalism and exhibits itself as the Catiline o*
Australian politics. It transparently opposes
the vital democratic plank of One-man-one-
vote by seeking to make its readers forget and
ignore the existing sectional conspiracy to
establish a dual vote—“ one vote for manhood
and one for thrift,” as the blundering and
plundering gag goes. Speaking about the arti-
ficial hole-and-corner “ push ” known as the
Victorian “country party,” the Age says :
The platform of this party may be said to consist of two
planks—the stock tax and retrenchment in the civil
service.
Not a word about its chiefest aim, the object
nearest the political gizzard of the scheming
politicians who have invented this “country
party ” —a dual vote for Proputty. However,
the workers of Victoria have no blind side on
which the Age can get in this vital matter.
Labour-member Hancock, carried Collingwood
in the teeth, and probably because of the
venomous opposition of the mighty penny daily,
and workers all over the province should be
prepared to show Dictator Same that his politi-
cal power vanished when lie deserted democracy
in order, to support syndicates, to boom monopo-
lies, and to overbalance the franchise of the
workers by giving secret aid to the duality of
the voting power of Property.
During the trial of W. P. Smairl, of Sydney,
last week for embezzlement, some curious facts
came out. One of them was the statement by
the bland and gentlemanly auditor that
“certified to the correctness of the accounts,’
but didn’t count the cash—which, as it hap-
pened, was £lOll 7s. 4d. short. The other
auditor, apparently, counted the cash and
never observed the shortage, so, between the
man who didn’t look at the deficiency and the
man who didn’t see it when he did look at it,
the shareholders of the Imperial Building
Society have gone where the good shareholder
goes —into the soup. A Melbourne society
which burst lately made even a worse dis-
covery : the borrowers’ ledger hadn’t been
written up for two years, and seemingly the
auditors had never observed the circumstance.
They professed, of course, to have gone over the
book and examined every separate entry, and
yet they weren’t even conscious that there were
no entries to examine. Apparently this kind
of an auditor doesn’t enter the office at all, but
simply hands in through the window his little
certificate stating that everything is quite
accurate, and has his fee thrown to him over
the back gate, and goes away quite content.
Wanted, ofir' 1 auditors, criminally liable for
neglect.

816 Maoquarie-street North, Sydney, Feb. 17,1892.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
If there is one thing in the world that makes me
smile behind my gauze fan, as depicted above
(I’ve had it a long time, haven’t I ?), it is to
hear the Sydney she-Suffragists crying aloud in the
wilderness, proclaiming their “advanced opinions”
—their peculiar aptitude for striking out in all sorts of
familiar directions-their stern contempt for the every-
day woman who has no Ideas - with a six-foot I. Because,
in sober truth, the Suffragists are a sheepy mob—one
woman’s opinion is their opinion, as anybody knows who
attends a Rights seance and watches the way the applause
goes. You have only to he “Lady” So-an’-So, and the
meeting lifts up both feet and its brass-shod gamp to
drive your namby-pambyisms home every time you open
your mouth—and what a Title disapproves of, the whole
surge of untrammelled feminine intellects disapprove of
at sight without asking any questions. It is pitiable.
More, it is sickening.
Apropos -at a recent pow-wow of the league the
lecturess rose on her Paris heels and wrestled with a
“ devil’s dozen ” of the objections agin the universal vote.
Some, she said, were too flippant to be heeded; for
instance, the reluctance of one woman to join in because
the members seemed (sonpturally enough) to take no
manner of heed wherewith they should be clothed. It
certainly was an objection to be counted out, for any
thinking woman would enlist under the banner of a scare-
crow with a commendable cause - but then only a handful
of people take the league seriously, and anything does for
an objection. A league canvasser told me that one woman
“didn’t think she would,” because she had to take two
trams to get to the place of worship, and another—a
woman, mind you—had the audacity to hack out
“because there was too much talking.” But, harking back,
the league does get itself up in a rather marked manner—
for business reasons, I suppose, just as a musician never
gets his hair clipped, and an artistalways sports a soft ’at.
But you can overdo eccentricity of outer shell. I re-
member herding up Hunter-street behind a leaguist once
who had caught all her waving petticoats too high at the
hack, and the result was a rather embarrassing perspec-
tive of cloudy white stockings ending in venerable boots ;
and when ray fascinated eye got to her bonnet I couldn’t
help wondering if the meeting had stood her on her head
in the corner for seditious utterances at the last stance.
Obviously women don’t want the franchise ; if they did
they would take a dfal more interest in municipal elec-
tions over which lady-householders are called upon by
local jacks-out-of-office and begged to vote them in again.
It is odd that when even the most companionable
member of Parliament starts in a train for anywhere, he
will descend to abject fibs to get a carriage to himself.
For instance, at Redfern the other day, a well-known
member hung out of a carriage-window, and told those
who approached that it was full to suffocation. They put
credence in his statement (you can’t tell members at
sight) and passed on, hut at last came a man who, seeing
there was room for three in the carriage, gently got in
and sat down. “You can’t sit there—that Walter’s seat,”
said the M.P. from his post of guard at the door, but the
stranger had been there before, and so only smiled fitfully
and settled himself comfortably—at least until Walter
came. But Walter never did come, and the journey was
a peaceful one after all, for the interloper had his seat
and was happy, and no M.P. is ever “ disconcerted” at
being bowled out in a little “ expediency ” like that.
Per printed report I gather that Melbourne Austral
Salon is still flourishing at the top of Uollins-street, with
a bank surplus, too, of three figures, and a sequential air
of joyousness in the region of the till. Yet I harbour a
ghastly dread that the Salon is slowly allowing its
appetite to become its god, for in the list of receipts,
innocently side by side, are the suggestive announce-
ments :
Sale of refreshments .. £9 17 7
Sale of stationery .... 56
Nine pounds twelve and a penny more for pastry than for
pens and ink!
Which reminds me that when the Manly flower-show
authorities sat down to cold, emotionless numerals the
other day, it transpired that there had been a loss on the
refreshments Of nine and a penny. However, I don’t
wonder at that. In a tea-and-cake tent given to amateur
waitresses in fetching clothes you always have to give
chase to the lady who waits upon you ere you can settle
with her. And everybody isn’t honest even at a flower-
show.
A lady just returned from ’Ome tells a story of an
English female, a frock-builder by trade, who had been
four years in India., coming aboard as a second-class
passenger at Colombo, and having to share a cahih with
some people returning to Adelaide. Almost ere the
steamer had started a female voice was heard expostulat-
ing on deck, and at last the new passenger burst in upon
the ship’s purser with the force of an enraged cyclone:
“Purser, I must have my cabin changed,” she gasped.
“ Why, what is the matter—isn’t there room where you
are?” “ Oh, yes, there is room enough,” said the lady
with acid emphasis, “ but I really am not accustomed to
‘•ravelling with Australians.”
Madame Weigal’s fashion fads for February enshrine
nothing which the woman with a now dress in her eye
will rush forth after a paper pattern of, but the sketches
of young ladies wear most of their dresses below high-
collar-bone mark, and I sincerely trust that isn’t a sign of
the times. For only one throat in a thousand will bear
unveiling, and the virgin who is no longer as young as
she would like to be simply hangs out the date of her
nativity when she allows her long lute-stringed gullet to
poke too far out of her collar.
Italian opera at Freetrade prices oped conspicuously at
Sydney Royal last Saturday. There wasn’t much “dress-
ing” in the circle, for this is the time when the d.c.
seraph’s garment generally reveals the dust of many
sweepings on the tip of its trail. Carrie Swain was in
white hroidered chiffon aggravated with diamonds and a
white plume fan, and was perhaps the most observable
personage. On the stage, Elvira wore three expensive
garments, but the women always do look guys in grand
opera because their frocks must be either chronological
or nondescript. A songstress can’t very well come out
dressed up to date to canoodle with a lover in doublet
and jack-boots, can she ? And if you dress to
enhance his antiquoness, it’s ten to one the femi-
nine fashions of a.d. ever so far back will only
make- the gallery “ chyak.” Therefore, Elvira wore
plain-cut non-committal garments—the first one of a sur-
prising greenness which made you think of Erin-go-
bragb; the second a white satin bridal dress (fortunately
worn without those yards and yards of mosquito-net
which make the stage-bird such a roundabout lump of
love) made perfectly plain and without ornament, and
the effect soothed. But in the third act (where Elvira
has a very bad time, you remember) to her black frock
was added a spray of flowers—a bit of coquetry no really
badgered female would ever dream of—for instance, don’t
you always doubt the grief of a widow who haunts hubby’s
little tomb on Sundays with carefully banged hair and a
white rose entangled in the buttons of her bodice ? La
Guidotti got a shower of floralities at the conclusion of
“Ernani Inviolami”—which reminds me that Ernani was
like a Valentine in scarlet and gold, and the two wicked
old gentlemen wore black necessarily. On the stage
people always wear black when they want to look old, or
virtuous—words sometimes synonymous.
In “Faust,” at Her Majesty’s, the actor-men have the
cream of the wardrobe—Jewett’s lovely get-up of blue
and gold brocade doublet, slashed with gold silk, worn
with electric-blue trunks and a cloak of electric-blue
plush, barred with velvet, was like something you dream
of, hut seldom see, except at a fancy-dress ball on a
pimply youth with champagne-bottle shoulders and
padded calves, and then, of course, you don’t notice it.
As the inflammable Marguerite, Kate Bishop (she gets
her face up to perfection, by the way) rang the changes
on virgin-white and electric-grey, slashed with mull—
only I must object to her sporting the same pair of
shoes from start to finish. They are dear little shoes
with glittering buckles, hut all the same one likes to think
that Maggie changed her hoots now and then. Mephisto.
(it sounds personal, but I must say that Mr. Rignold
makes a realistic devil) glowed like a live coal in scarlet
and black, and the yanking up’ards of Marguerite to the
strains of Heavenly music was charmingly done. By the
way, why is Heavenly music always soprano, while the
melodies of Tartarus are set in the bars clef ? Is there
any visible connection between virtue and treble register?
I think not. No, dear, I think not.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The conjunction of Jupiter and Venus was
to happen on Saturday evening last, and did in
all probability eventuate as predicted, but owing
to the haziness of the sky it was invisible.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “ Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street,is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
condnoted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

but those ladies who wish to save time, labour,

Why ? There is no handle to turn, not half so
much ice used, and it keeps the cream firm in or
out doors. Two quarts of ice cream can be made
for Is. fid. to Is. l)d. Send for Sydney testimonials
to T. A. Knight & Co., St. James Chambers,
King-street, Sydney, who supply the machine.
Price, 80s., with instructions so simple that a
child can understand.
All in a clear and cloudless sky,
To Venus bright came Jove a-wooing,
And boldly in her path he twirled,
Her brilliant presence still pursuing.
And she, the cunning, artful minx,
Came gaily dancing up before him,
Pretending coldness, when, in truth.
Just for a time she did adore him.
Now Jove—oh, tell it not in Gath,
Nor to the Philistines reveal it—
From Venus meant to gain one kiss,
And, if she would not give it, steal it.
And some old fogey, in his zeal,
Told all us mortals here below,
And Mrs. Grundy and her crew
All toddled out to see the show.
But, Jove (e’en in his lightest mood,
He’s very stately, stern and wise),
Just dropped a heavy curtain down,
And hid that kiss from prying eyes.
M.C.H.

On Saturday last at Sydney Royal the
Italian Opera Company opened a four weeks’
season with “Ernani.” The house was crowded,
and the treasurer sat, like Matthew, at the
receipt of custom, a dazzling vision of glittering
gems and snowy shirt-front. There is some
subtle and occult connection between diamonds
and Italian opera, the mystery of which we are
still engaged in unravelling. Likewise, no one
who is anyone—unless he happen to be a press-
man or a policeman—dares sit amidst the elect in
the dress-circle in any other than the garb of the
waiter, for it is impossible to understand Verdi’s
music unless you are sitting awkwardly and self-
consciously on a crumpled swallow-tail. However,
there was a great deal of enthusiasm pervading
the atmosphere ; floral tributes—not necessarily
provided by the management —were occasionally
hurled with manifestations of delirious joy at
Signorina Guidotti, much delight was expressed
by hand-clappiug, several press people appeared
solemn and ostentatiously read opera-scores, and
Potts’ Point and Elizabeth Bay looked highly
edified and unspeakably bored, and wondered
what the deuce it was all about, you know.
# # *
“Ernani” is a sad and solemn thing to
contemplate. Its main interest is the pursuit of
a lady, named Elvira, by three bearded pirates—
one of whom is a bandit, and another a king,
though there isn’t much difference —and the sur-
render of a penny trumpet upon which bangs a
life. Some men wither and are gathered to their
fathers at a breath of air—poor Ernani wilts sadly
and hands in his checks at the blast of a bugle.
There is also a tomb in the piece, and taking it all
round it is moderately cheerful and eminently
reassuring, Signorina Guidotti is a magnificent
Elvira of superb dimensions, whom it is impossible
to behold without loving. She is a Spaniard, we
are told, though if you didn’t see her you would
conclude that she was a nightingale. The Ernani
of this production is also a Spaniard. On the
play-bill he is called a dramatic tenor. He acts
fairly well, but he strains too much after effect in
his singing. He forgets that “ Ernani ” was
written purely in the interests of “ Carlo Magno,”
King of Spain. Signor Melossi, the potentate in
question, is a fine baritone with a powerful and
sympathetic voice, and he may fairly be considered
as having all the unguent of the show, although a
little falls to the basso, Signor Travaglini, who
lias a stage presence reminiscent of Bondi,
The conductor, Signor Napoleone Maffezzoli,
works like a veritable Trojan, and has a
capital orchestra well under his control. The
principals and chorus are well dressed, and the
different productions are admirably mounted and
staged, and altogether so cheap a presentation of
operas of the calibre now given to the public
could not be witnessed in Italy itself. By the
way, touching the tomb-,scene —when Pimazzoni
played “Carlo Magno,” he emerged from the
grisly vault in a dazzling suit of silver mail, and
the effect was simply electric, the conspirators
appearing to be genuinely justified in taking the
tin-plated monarch for the ghost of the defunct
Charlemagne. On Tuesday, “ Lucia di Laniraer-
moor ” was produced with an entirely different
cast, the personnel of which shall have our par-
ticular attention in a future issue,
* * * *
At Sydney Her Majesty’s, “Faust” lias
replaced the anonymous drama of “Forty-Nine,”
and Maggie Moore dances no more the sand-jig
of her sires. “Faust” is a very fine production.
The painting of the cathedral leaves nothing to be
desired, and the incidental music and the lime-
light are exceedingly good of their kind. Every
child should be taken to see “Faust” at the
earliest opportunity, for it is calculated to destroy
all fear or reverence for the “ Bogey Man ” men
have agreed for several centuries to pile their sins
upon. Mr. George Rignold’s devil is hale and
fat, and he cannot get about very rapidly. He
has a low comedy nose and an American goatee.
His laugh belongs to the bold, bad man of modern
melodrama, and he behaves generally as a cheap
conjuror in a dime museum, rather than a renegade
angel with serious business on hand. Goethe
created a splendid fiend who clothed nis spirit
with the thoughts of evil that existed in the minds
of those he tempted. He assumed the guise they
wished, or their own peculiar wicked desires sug-
gested. Mr. Gilbert Horatio Parker has travestied
and tortured Goethe’s sublime creation into a
mountebank in a red cloak, with a transpontine
laugh, who wanders about market-places mesmer-
ising little children and setting beggars by the
ears. Mr. Rignold’s Mephistopheles is consistent
throughout. Moreover, it is a clever piece of act-
ing, but after witnessing it we feel it would be a
solemn farce henceforth to consign anybody to the
devil. He can hardly be blamed for this. He
hired Mr, Parker to manufacture him a fiend,
and Mr. Parker was sparing of the material.
The author, an ex-elergyman, by the way,
hadn’t wickedness enough to clothe his ideas
in, so he made good the deficiency in limp
and laugh and limelight. After Mr. Rignold
is taken out there is hardly anybody left in this
particular edition of “ Faust not even the call-
boy. Poor Marguerite is, of course, played by
Miss Bishop, and very charmingly and pathetically
does she sustain the part, notwithstanding the
fact that she is hardly the sylph-like girl that
Goethe dreamed about. There is a change in the
Faust. It is now played by Mr. Jewett, for
which we are devoutly thankful: have we not yet
rasping in our ears the voice of brawn of Mr.
Brian Barley’s Faust ? Mr. Stirling Whyte has
let us into the secret of the German doctor’s
nationality—while he was an old man Faust was
Scotch ; when he renewed his youth he became,
in the person of Mr. Jewett, pronouncedly Aus-
tralian. A word of praise is deserved by Mr.
Hazlitt for his fine and manly acting of the
character of Valentine. Likewise the man who
runs the limelight sticks to his post and does his
duty like a hero.
* * * *

*****
It would not surprise us to learn that the
tug-of-war strike was suggested to the competing
teams by the associated theatrical managers of
Sydney, who are now put very much in the same
position as they were by the Pain’s fireworks
exhibitions. Parallel the risks: the theatrical
manager is obliged to lease an expensive theatre
for a term of months or years, to engage stars at
high salaries, to provide an orchestra, a staff of
carpenters, scene-painters, house-hands and ticket-
takers, and to become responsible for the whole
enormous expenditure of running a large establish-
ment with no other certainty of success than popular
caprice. The tug-of-war promoter, on the other
hand, is obliged to rent a hall for a series of per-
formances stretching over only a week or ten days,
to hire an ordinary band, and to put up two or
three hundred pounds prize-money (which, con-
sidering the novelty and the minimum of risk and
expense, he should be able to borrow on the
security of a percentage of the takings). Four
prizes only are offered, and all the beaten team?
are practically put in the position of providing the
public with amusement at their own personal loss
—this means 80 men engaged night after night
with no possible prospect of remuneration.
Theatrical managers never get a “ throw in ” such
as this. The promoters of the tug of-war urge,
however, that the men should compete solely from
motives of patriotism. Patriotism is evidently
becoming one of the last refuges of management.
** * *
At the Opera House, on Monday night,
George Musgrove’s variety show was received
with mingled feelings of astonishment and pain.
Naturally, the Graggs raised a genuine furore by
the skill with which they tossed their unwhole-
some small boy from foot to foot. The Graggs’
aot throughout was recognised as the perfection of
muscular art, also Carl Hertz, a trickster of
ren wn. opened the eyes of everybody to every-
thing save the way in which it was done, although
some of the charm of his rather protracted enter-
tainment is lost in a large theatre. Of the so-
called illusions, invented by Hertz, the newest is
not an illusion strictly speaking, inasmuch as the
metamorphosis of a man into a girl is perpetrated
behind curtains. What the eye can’t see, the optic
can’t be deluded by. It was with the incidental
performances of Fred. Mason, Rose Bearing aim
the Fairy Four that the pain came in. As items
in a music-hall bill or a pantomime they fid a .
want, but when they lack the glamour of a go l '
geous scene and have to face an audience who
can’t appeal to a bottle between the verses these
artists seem sad and wearisome. They will not
bear considering in cold blood, for which reason
mainly, but also because of a general want or
briskness, the new show as a whole fell some-
what flat on Monday. A comic ballet brought
matters to a fairly cheerful conclusion, but the
congregation dispersed in an ominously quiet and
unobtrusive manner. ,

“La Cigale,” which duly put in a &’ st
southern appearance at the Princess’s, on Satur-
day, promises to remain the show of Melbourne
for several weeks to come. The fierce light that
beats upon a comic opera reveals much that is
rich and rare in the production of this latest
novelty, and regarded as a feast for the eye it 19
verily a sumptuous repast. Six fair dancers,
assisted by one dark ditto, triumph over many
difficulties with their legs, alike in breezy rura
districts and in the highly perfumed salon, where
they trip to the strains of an orchestra of ladm 9 ,
who fiddle away like ministering angels m ,
balcony built for that purpose. There is dancing)
too, in the market-place, all amongst the orange
peel and nut-shells, and here a nimble little fay
constructed on the lightest possible lines, make-
the greybeards wag with admiration. Moreover
and more particularly, the music of coinp°®. e

Vow, tho'moral English version of “La Cigale”

With Miss Marie Halfcon rested the ques-
tion ’twixt success or failure for “La Cigale,” and
that gifted new arrival decided in favour of the
former with scarce a moment’s hesitation. The
lady’s Parisian experience is delightfully apparent,
especially in a “kiss” song, which reduced the
whole house to ruins at her feet in the first act.
No ditty so cleverly interpreted as this had been
previously heard under the same sliding roof. It
is as a singing actress that Miss Halton in all her
moods soars high above the average, and creates
her most refreshing effects. Her well-trained
voice is not very strong, nor does she talk to any
particular advantage, but the intelligence of her
warble is quite unusual. Personally, Miss Halton
inclines to stoutness in moderation, though
indeed, her face, which is pretty, would bear
(fining down. Suppose we say that her general
appearance suggests an improved edition of Sig-
nora Cuttica, whilst her bearing and gestures are
strongly reminiscent of Isabel Morris. Anyway,
she is a solid success. Howard Vernon plays the
absurd dook in a style that is comparatively
fresh and new to him, and certainly carries off
second prize. Ryley, as the Chevalier, is happy
and straightforward as usual. There is never much
attention to petty details about Ryley’,s acting, but
he seems to stroll afteT the ladies as though he
liked it, and it’s always nice to see a good sort of
fellow enjoying himself. Besides, they’ve got him
up regardless of expense, and his best pair of
breeches is a twin brother to the plush curtain.
Florence Young, without a ballad to call her own,
acts the duchess prettily, and there the interest
ends. Elton, trying to brew humour of bosh, was
a dire infliction on Saturday, and will require to
touch up his part very skilfully if he wants to win
-genuine laughs. Flora Graupner gets an occa-
sional peep in, and kauri, Lissant, Elsie Cameron
■ and Violet Varley hover around at times. Allen
Morris, the new baritone, shaped unobtrusively in
.a colourless part, and as his voice, for some reason
iorother, refused to “come off,” his first appear-
ance made no difference worth mentioning. “La
'Cigale” is an opera without a tenor, and would
deserve enthusiastic support if there were nothing
else in its favour,

“ The Hobby Horse ” took a picrimiuary
canter at the Bijou, on Saturday night, and its
style of going was not encouraging to backers.
Pinero turns out hopeless plays every now and
then, crazy works of art that caricature human
nature without being comical, and seem to defy
probability out of pure cussedness. And he has
the late Mr. Boucicault’s weakness for creating
sporting characters when he knows nothing of the
sporting world. In “ Dandy Dick ” the author’s
extravagance in horsey terms and conceptions was
justified by results, but “The Hobby Horse”
hasn’t enough result to justify anything. It
w a dreary outcome of the Ibsen period, a
tiresome three-act enlargement on the simple
tact that a husband who sticks to racing has
nothing in common with a wife who sticks
’° Professional charity work, and that a
mutual sacrifice of manias is necessary to bring
their souls within speaking distance. - An attempt
to blend their fads in a religious Home for Decayed
Jockeys and blacklegs fails miserably, partly, no
uoubt, on account of the residents being an insult
t° all understanding. There is one touch of real
mature in “ The Hobby Horse.” When the lead-
'll" lady endeavours to fulfil her mission under the
t'rectum of a curate, the gentleman in holy orders

xception there is nothing satisfactory about the

At Poverty Point.
The husband of Mrs. Kirby, whose son attempted her
life by throat-cutting the other night, used to keep the
theatrical bookselling shop under the “Old Vic.’ in
Pitt-st., Sydney, and was burnt out when the playhouse
went. His wife assisted in the shop and was well up in
dramatic lore. Dr. Muskett, who was called in to attend
Mrs. Kirby, is himself the son and grandson of the earliest
booksellers in Melbourne.
W. H. Pennington took his farewell of the English stage
the other day. As an actor Pennington, who has been
“ starring ” at odd times at out of the way places for
many years, was a solemn and terrible character, but he
was blessed with a useful advt. in the fact that he had
been a Balaclava ’ero. On every anniversary of that
little event Pennington is engaged somewhere or other,
to recite the Charge of the Light Brigade. The gas is
lowered and the gallant ex-dragoon mouths Tennyson’s
poem in a most impressive manner. This performance
grows more humanly interesting as the Balaclava muddle
fades into ancient history, and very soon, perhaps, old
Penny will stand in his pride alone, the sole surviving
relic of all the “ glory.” “ Honour the charge I made ! ”
he will be able to say.
Elton (says a correspondent) is not going “home”
because he has any particular love for Hingland. That
affection he had once, though, and it was cooled in this
way. He returned from the Capo, some years back,
expecting to be received as a martyr in London. But he
fell flat, nobody welcomed him, and most of the theatres
were closed. On the fifth day of his second advent he
met an old face, which nodded and went its way. “By
,” said Billy to his wife, “this won’t do.” He
followed the acquaintance (a well-known manager) and
grabbed him by the collar. Manager, coolly : “ So you’ve
returned from America, old boy.” Billy: “No.”
Manager; “From India?” Billy;' “No.” Manager:
“ Well then, from Australia.” “And I’m damned,” says
Billy, “if I hadn’t to tell him that I’d been at the Cape.
That cured me of my nostalgia.” It cost Billy £3OO to
bring his family to Australia. This is too much, audit
has been his desire for a long time to settle the wife and
youngsters in a suburb of London. When this is done, he
says, lie will either accept an engagement in London, or
turn his eyes to America.
Maculiy, professor of elocution, provided a rare trea*
for the boys at Clara Merivale’a benefit. Mac’s sample o*
elocution was probably correct enough in small details"
but as his voice didn’t travel beyond the orchestra the
trouble was to find out whether the worthy man was try-
ing to melt ’em to tears, or set the theatre in a roar. The
theatre roared, anyway, when the gods began to ac-
company the professor’s dumb show with whistling
variations on “ Home, Sweet Home.” It was a painfully
humorous exhibition whilst it lasted, but Mao. took his
ironical call with apparent complacency, and a few lady
admirers, who had tripped in specially to hear the
“ master” recite, seemed much gratified at his success.
In the London advertisement of “Cinder Ellen” vve
read; “The audience is politely requested to be seated at
eight o’clock, as the plot of the piece terminates at
quarter-past.”
The stalls of one Sydney theatre must have been built
for men with detachable wooden legs. A far-seeing and
bountiful Providence has blessed the average “colonial”
young man with a pair of lovely nether limbs. How the
doose can a chappie impress his girl when be sits beside
her like a trussed kangaroo and gazes at the show through
the chink between his knees?
The late Alfred Cellier left, by will, the “rights” of
“ The Mountebank ’ to Nellie Stewart.
Lucy Escott and Henry Squires, of the old Lyster
Opera Troupe, enjoy a happy old age in a villa on the
shores of the Mediterranean. The lady’s name is Lucy
E. Scott.
The Dick Lee-W. G. Grace letter brings to memory the
Squires episode of many years ago. Thu “silvery tenor”
had written to a friend in ’Frisco, speaking of the success
of the Opera Co. in Melbourne. He used the phrase—“ I
like this shore, but everything and everybody is blawsted
English.” The letter was published in the ’Frisco papers,
and found its way into the Melbourne journals. Lyster’s
troupe, then in Adelaide, S.A., were billed for a coining
season in Melbourne, opening with “ Martha.” The house
was crowded, and Squires' reception anything hut plea-
sant. The d.c. applauded to the echo, the other parts
howled, hissed and hooted. Farquharson, a great
favourite, came to the front, placed his hand upon Squires’
shoulder, and made a short explanation. The opera was
allowed to go on with occasional interruptions, but when
Squires sang “The Last Rose of Summer,” the house rose
at him, and he had no further need of an apologist.
Sara Bernhardt went bear-shooting when in the U.S.,
and killed two bears single-handed. They were old,
tame bears with most of the hair rubbed off them, and
they were bought from a local saloon-keeper tor the
occasion, no wild ones being available. Some of Sara’s
admirers led the dejected animals out into the woods and
chained them up, and then the divine tragedienne either
shot them or pounded them to death with the thick end
of her gun amid great rejoicings.
Zola’s new play, “ Therese Raquin,” is said to be a sort
of epileptic screech—a horror in four nightmares with a
moral. A woman, assisted by her lover, murders her
husband to clear the way for their marriage. The
woman’s paralysed mother-in-law haunts them ever after-
wards ; she knows the details of their crime, and only her
paralysis keeps her from denouncing them, but she
her baleful green eye upon them, like the starboard light
of a steamer, and ultimately that oppressive optic drives
them to suicide.
Musgrove and Williamson are on the best of terms,
evidenced by the former lending his scenic artist, Gordon
to J.O. W., to co-operate with Goatcher, in “ La Cigale.”
The Melbourne Knights of the Bucket - make the gruel
thick and slab—complain of a superfluity of flour and
water. In other words, managers have been cutting
down the bills this way and that way till the .esthetic
education of bill-boarding is all behind. Look at “ Evan-
geline”—a few miserable slips, while every hoarding
blossomed with the magnificent pictures when the two
Macs, were at their apex, zenith, and apogee.
Billee Barlow, sick, was out of the Melbourne “ Whit-
tington” cast six nights, but has re-appeared, whereupon
the first mention appears in the press of her being away.
Now, this is wrong, and indicates a fault in the critics,
perceptible in a variety of Melbourne cases recently. They
ought not to allow managers to dictate to them in
these matters, and here are other recent cases of the same
thing in Melbourne—no mention of substitution till the
principal re-appeared: Dauby, eight nights out of “Ruy
Bias ” ; Nellie Farren, about a week from the same piece ;
G. Rignold, as long from the “ Merry Wives.” So much
for your “ impartial press.”
Pattie Brown talks of going to England in April.
Irving’s “ Henry VIII.” is a tremendous affair. Pre-
liminary cost, £15,000, wherefore no profit can possibly be
handled, even with crowded houses, the first ten weeks.
Thereafter the ex.’s will go on, at £2OO a night, while the
packed house is £4OO. Ravenswood was a failure, and
Irving must be always tremulous over his immense
ventures, knowing how Bulwer’s posthumous “ Junius ”
ruined Wilson Barrett. Brough and Boucicault’s “Much
Ado ” won’t really begin to pay till Sydney sees it.
London Referee finds Horace Lingard’s production of
“ La Fauvette ” to be somewhat “ country tied and unso-
phisticated,” a cheap and roughly-finished article, in
fact. Also, the critic complains that there is too much
Horace Lingard, who “ takes the centre of the stage as if
it belonged to him as indeed it does in a manner of
speaking, for Mr. Lingard is ins own manager, and simply
does as he pleases.”
The past fortnight, writes our London correspondent
by last mail, lias made a record in primih'es : Haddon
Chambers’ “The Honourable Herbert,” at the Vaude-
ville ; Comyns Carr’s “Forgiveness,” at the St. .James';
Sydney Grundy’s “A Fool’s Paradise,” at the Garrick;
Gilbert and Cellier's “ The Mountebanks,” at the Lyric:
and “ Cinder Ellen," at the Gaiety. Add to these Irving’s
magnificent revival of “Henry VIII.,” the opening of
“ Venice in London," and Gus. Harris's big pantomime,
and you have an unparalleled list. “ A Fool’s Parad.se ”
is, of exiurse, not absolutely new, but it has never before
been properly staged and acted- It is drawing the town.
The annual ceremony of cutting the “ Baddeley Cake,”
writes our correspondent, was performed for the first time
/or many years somewhat in accordance with the old
actors’ intentions,, in the green-room of old Drury in the
presence of the company at present playing there.
Harris, however, not content with this, arranged a some-
what similar ceremony across the road at Covcnt Garden,
whither be had bidden the usual crowd of bang.ws oi:
Shortly after 11.80 the great man mounted a dais and
uncovered a huge cake which he proceeded to cut,
announcing that it was to lie known as “the Harris.”
