The London critics agree, remarks our correspondent,
that Olga Nethersoie’s acting lias improved out of recog-
nition since she went to Australia. She played in “A
Pool’s Paradise” with great power, and contributed as
much as Hare himself towards tire undoubted success of
tjie play. Young Irving, in a thankless part, shows
improvement, and as he is ridding himself of his melo-
dramatic manner there is still some hope for the youth.
Young Hare lias already proved that the stage is his
metier: his career is assured.
“ Billy ” Florence was very near-sighted—couldn’t see
anyone in the auditorium, for which reason ho acted
better. Buokstone was so deaf that he had to watch the
mouths of the other performers.
The tiff between Moody and Sankey was because Sankey
took what is called a royalty on American organs, popu-
larised by ins playing.
Lawrence’s new “Life of G. V. Brooke ” is candid on
his feminine entanglements. He long travelled with
Madame Marie Duret, who came to Australia, but was
never here associated with him. When Brooke started
from London for Melbourne another lady was with him,
apparently not an actress, and recognised everywhere as
Mrs. Brooke, who kept house for him in Australia,
receiving all ins guests. Thus Miss Fanny Oathcart, a
strict young lady, could come to Australia as Mr.
Brooke’s pupil. When she arrived a very important
manager, who had buried two wives, fell in love with her,
but Boh Heir carried off the prize, and there was a bit of
a rumpus—soon healed.

Aunts,”

King of Bavaria with an elegant gold star that twinkle®
the name of “Hertz” in diamonds. This testimony to
genius is slightly discounted by the fact that His Majesty
was off his chump.
Miss Adey Brunei, who is certainly a winsome and un-
conventional reciter, seems to be coming forth as a
teacher of elocution in Melbourne, to which end the fair
lady advertises a number of extravagant press_ notices
from “ home.” Some kind friend had better advise Miss
A. B. that English newspaper eniogium doesn't count for
much over here—nor over there, either. Besides, she has
already appeared at two Melbourne matinees and given
more proof of her ability than could be gathered from a.
mile of stereotyped praise by professional British
gushers.
Max O’Bell arrives in Sydney by the April Yankee
mail.
At Hobart, Wirth and Co. lately gave some open-air
cow-boy exhibitions at a sports ground. Of the two
audiences assembled—one inside and the other outside
the ground—the former was mainly composed of poor
people while the outsiders were a well-dressed lot.

The Gaiety Company have introduced J. F. Sheridan’s
“Little Glass” song into “Cinder Ellen” in London,

Meston has ail his myalls ready for collecting when pre-
pared to start on his American and European tour. He
will take 25 picked men and gins, including five of the
famous “Kalkadoonas,” one of whom is nearly 7ft. high.
His partner, B. H. Purcell, is bringing these in from the
M'Kinlay Ranges. Meston will take one of the finest
ethnological collections in Australia. He intends to give
special lectures before ail the scientific societies in the
countries visited, and will stay some time at the Chicago
Exposition.

brother Harry, Frank Emery, and Lohr.
Marie Halton, Williamson’s new Cigaie, has struck a
grand blow for popularity. Williamson could hardly ask
La Cigaie to act for the unemployed, but she overheard
the talk, and came forward generously with the proffer.
What is becoming of the poor stage? People com-
plained of a clever woman singing an indifferent song, in
the Melbourne pantomime now running, and were told it
was an advertisement! In the burlesque at another
theatre there is a big whisky advertisement, in the prin-
cipal scene, but it was hoped the like of this had ever
been banished the harlequinade.

“ La Basoche.”

business in Tasmania, where his varied abilities were
greatly admired.
Harry Jewett meditates matrimony.
A London society paper declares that the Actors’ Asso-
ciation has done nothing to justify its existence, except
to take the subscription moneys of actors and actre-sts,
many of whom can ill spare the cash. It has never prose-
cuted bogus managers, nor moved in the matter of
insanitary dressing-rooms. It made a feeble splutter
against some agents, and is now anout to start a theatrical
paper. Sookanbuskin ! The actor is to be his own critic.
A ballet shame —women in tights.
Another story about deceased dramatist Wills. Like
other geniuses he had a strong distaste for clean linen,
and a friend meeting him one day said: “Dear hoy, I
had a terrible fright last night. Thought I saw a ghost,
and was in such a funk I turned as white as your shirt.”
Then he glanced at Wills’s dicky and added, “By Jove,
old man, I turned a good deal whiter than your shirt.”
George Anson will have several good stories to tell of
“ the colonies ” when he goes Home. One of his favourites
is about an excursion he and a party of merry mummers
took up the Nepean when he was last with the B. and B.
company in Sydney. The scenery was new to them, and
at each successive turn in the river the actors expressed
their admiration by such exclamations as, “ Bravo,
Gordon,” “ Well done, Spong,” “Good man, Brunton”
(scenic artists, you know). At last a specially fine view
came in sight, and a hitherto silent gentl man in the
stern gravely remarked, “Bravo, Mon Dieu!” Either
the man who said that was French, or he had borrowed
the remark from a Frenchman.
Miss Dwight, the well-known Melbourne Temperance
Hall pianiste, died last week.
A Brunswick (Melb.) shepherd held up his hands in
holy horror last week. He had assisted an unemployed
widow with a large family, and next day one of her little
boys told him—“ Oh, we had such a jolly time! Mother
took us to the Circus I” Moral : Never assist the Un-
employed.
“Bail Up,” the drama written for Mrs. Keightly to
immortalise her ride from Rockley to Bathurst, N.S.W.,
to obtain from her father 500 sovs. to rescue her husband,
the late 11. M. Keightly, from a bushranging gang, has
been produced at Bathurst, where the audience, knowing
the facts, found the author’s fiction very disappointing.
Morever, they didn’t quite like to see the lady making
capital out of an event which cost one man ids life and
very nearly cost the late husband his. In the play Ben.
Hall is depicted ns a blood-thirsty scoundrel, whereas he
never attempted to take life, and is supposed to have been
a rather mild-mannered sort of highwayman.

The red flag—which means defiance, just
as the white flag means peace and the- black
piracy—has been hugely advertised in Melbourne
through good Pastor Bevan—salary A3O weekly—
having refused to speak under it, at the Sunday
unemployed meeting. The Pastor’s red hag is
his nice, soft, red velvet pulpit-cushion which he
hangs out every Sunday.
: The S.A. Surveyor-General has sent to
the museum an egg, depressed at one end, and
also oppressed with the photograph of a snake.
It is learnedly argued that the hen-mother was
terrified by a snake whose image she lithographed
maternally, after the fashion in which Jacob fooled
PTncle Laban in the matter of the ewes at water-
ing-time. Fancy and fable are not yet dead.
When Booth arrived in London, after his
tour of the world, the Salvationists hired 10
steamers, and crowded into them 4100 blatant
howlers armed with trombones* bassoons, cat
calls, hymns, saokbnts, bagpipes, and ail manner
of horrors. There was a big bill to pay for the
steamers and the whooping, but it all came out of
the money that the Australian believer gave for
the poor. We are going to yell for the Pope after
this. He seems the cheaper institution of the
two.
The Institute of Journalists, which hopes
to be “Australian,” compiled its constitution a
few nights ago in Melbourne. There are to be
associates, fellows, members and pupil-members.
Women “possessing tire necessary qualifications ”
will be admitted. One good feature about the
provisional council is that they are, almost all,
men whose noses are at the grindstone to-day and
against whom there is not a breath of suspicion.
Chief Justice Higinbotham has promised to be
president. Next general meeting on 27th.
Some Newcastle (N.S.W.) small tradesmen
have just been fined 255. each and costs under the
Towns Police Act (2 Vic. No. 2, sect. 10) for the
hideous offence of selling a glass of milk on Sun-
day. That a law which makes it a crime for an
honest man who has sixpence in his pocket to buy
a drink of milk and a bunch of grapes for himself
and his child, should be still extant, is surprising ;
that about 50 per cent of the clergy should support
it only shows what a set of cantankerous idiots
have forced their services on the almighty.
Another fact about Dick Lee. author of
the W. G. Grace lampoon. He arrived on Be ndigo
just as the Independent editor was looking round
to see a man, wherefore Dick went that very night
to report a political meeting, crowded and im-
portant, where Hattam, and a number of others,
held forth with a flood of loyalty. Dick was in a
floody state, but produced a large batch of copy.
Next day a shower of letters poured into the
office, but Dick was about as far as Castlemainc.
In short, every speaker complained he was
abominably misrepresented, and that the-report
was a stream of seditious lava. The editor
published one or.two letters, adding “We have
received other letters to the same tenor, and regret
that we have been deceived and imposed upon.’’
All very funny, no doubt, very droll and ex-
travagantly comic, to read about at this time of
day.
Melbourne telegrams state in a low tone
of voice that Superintendent Kennedy “does not
agree” with “ the finding of the board appointed
to inquire into the Edward Bell case.” Consider-
ing the language which that board used about
Kennedy this statement seems too probable to be
worth mentioning. We never yet knew any
accused individual who didn’t fail to agree when
the Judge made unpleasant remarks about him,
whether the remark was “ten years” or a five
shilling fine. Meanwhile Detective O’Donnell is
to be “ transferred.” What the board said about
him was practically that he obtained the convic-
tion of an innocent man, knowing him to be
innocent, and now he is to shift his peculiar
talent up to Stawell, where there probably are a
lot of innocent men badly in want of conviction.
Dear Bulletin, Your correspondent
“ Kyalite,” whose letter on the Riverine eel ques-
tion appears in your issue of to-day, is certainly
complimentary when he asks me to admit that I
don’t know a small eel from a leech. I have
asserted, and still assert, that on the occasion
mentioned in my previous letter I saw thousands
of small eels lying dead on the sandbanks of the
Edwards, and even the unique experience of
“ Kyalite,” who says he has fished that river as
man and boy for 30 years without seeing any eel.
either small or large, proves nothing in the way
of a direct negative. “ Kyalite ” simply says that
he has never seen or heard of eels being in the
Edwards, and I say positively that I have seen
thousands of small ones. These little eels were
exact facsimiles of large fresh-water eels, except
that they were of a delicate pink colour. If
“Kyalite” takes the trouble to make inquiries
among the townspeople of Deniliquin, who went
lobster-catching when the river became black on
the occasion to which I have referred, he will
doubtless find plenty of persons who can confirm
my statement. —H. 8., Sydney, Feb. 13, 1802.
P.'S.—l notice “Kyalite” says Ac caught leeches
in the Edwards. Is he sure they weren’t eels?
Dear Bulletin, —One of the games of the
land-monopolist (I include the big selector) In the
district of Narrabri West, N.S.W., is to fence in
the Government reserves, or, when they do ribt
fence them in, they graze their sheep and cattle
upon them, stealing the grass that belongs to the
public, and economising that in their own pad-
docks. Carriers find it difficult, and at times
impossible, to discover a decent feeding-camp for
their bullocks and horses, for the reserves arc
eaten bare by the usurping stock of the very men
for whom the carriers are expected to.take heavy
loads along ugly routes. Worse ; Carriers have
in places been charged agistment fees of 3d. per
night per head of stock for depasturing their
animals on Government reserves that have been
fenced in by the predatory squattah. Worst of
all; Carriers’ teams have actually been impounded
from the said reserves. Is this legal? A carrier
generally has no money to fool away in lawsuits,
so he puts up with those extortions. Besides, he
would bo boycotted from employment if he
resisted the encroachments of the men with whom
he makes carrying-contracts. Will so*io member
of Parliament bring this grievancp under the
notice of fbe Minister for Lands ? —Yours, Ac., D.

officially declared that its chief object is to defeat
any attempt to secure for one section of the com-
munity the power to dominate the remainder.
Therefore, ds chief objectis to commit suicide.

the Melbourne Criminal Sessions, the solemn
striking of convictees off the Electoral Roll, but

That smellful politician, John Davies,
M.L.C., is now an out-and-out Tory. J. Davies
reminds us of the scotched snake which vainly
tries" to bite its own tail. With this difference —
that though Davies’ political backbone is fractured
he succeeds in biting his own tail.
The unmistakable Melbourne feeling is
chat either Berry should be sent back as Agent-
General, or M. H. Davies should have the office —
anything rather than bungling Munro. Davies
would really be the next best man to Berry—who
should be packed to England again instanter.
W. R. Campbell’s reason for desiring to
enfranchise the “copper” is artless and to the
point—“ They raise families and there is not a
more Conservative body in the country than the
police.” A Tory who multiplies his species ought
to be encouraged. By the way, almost every man
in a uniform is a Tory.
A meeting of Foreign-traders was held on
Thursday last week in Sydney Carington Hall to
protest against the Customs Duties Bill, The
Telegraph states that the hall was full; the
S.M. Herald mentions sadly that about 50 people
were present. Evidently one of them doesn’t
know the truth when it sees it.
There’s a political lightning-change artiste
in Melbourne. He went in the Liberal interest
against Hancock, for Collingwood ; then became
fugleman for the “National” Association, on
salary, as he admitted at a public meeting; and
now he’s Liberal again, going for Collingwood,
“ because the association wouldn’t agree to One-
man-one-vote.” Oh, g’long !

The “ country party ” in the Victorian
Assembly have constructed their new “ platform,”
coiuprising eleven planks of an A.B.C. character,
with “additional recommendations.” Plank 3 is
“ In favor of immediate and vigorous measures
being taken to re-establish public confidence, and
to restore, the financial equilibrium.” Somehow
this reminds us of the suppositious politician who
declared himself a staunch advocate of increased
prosperity and better times.
“General” Booth says that “the pros-
perity of the Australian colonies is of mushroom
growth, and is of such a nature that it cannot
blend with religion.” Australia is a toadstool and
not an edible mushroom to Booth and exploiters of
his kind. Though he found many monopolists
enamoured of his cheap-slavery proposals, the
political power of the masses, who are self-pro-
tectively opposed to his “ submerged - tenth ”
scheme, was too great an obstacle for the self-
elected Pope of the Slums.

Melbourne Argus is straining its loins to
bring about a second Service-Berry coalition, this
time with Service as Premier in the Upper House,
with guasi-neutral addenda in the persons of
“Sir” Henry Wrixon, J. G. Duffy, M‘Lean, &c.
An “impossible” scheme. Service would want to
be Treasurer, and the Assembly wouldn’t stand
an M.L.C. in that niche, besides which, such a
location of the Treasury portfolio would be
distinctly unconstitutional and anti-democratic.
Further, Service is now old and feeble —over
seventy, and loves to be coddled by his women
folk.
The attempt of Trickett, M.L.C. (N.S.W.),
to deprive of the franchise all police and military
servants, stipendiary magistrates, clerks of petty
sessions, &c., cannot be justified by any reason-
able logic. If Trickett were logical, lie would
aspire to higher flights and disqualify all judges
and even legislators—aye, and the most potent
army of bailiffs and the common hangman. Free-
dom would indeed shriek at any such a travestied
injustice. Speaking soberly, manhood suffrage is
neither more nor less than what the common
interpretation of the words mean. Constables are
men, therefore they are entitled to vote. They
may have official as well as private consciences,
but the ballot only recognises their right as men.
Whether the constable polls his official or bis
private conscience is not a wrinkle on the political
welkin.
David Christie Murray, speaking recently
at a Colonial Institute meeting, struck a great
patch of truth as under :
I lamented, and I still lament the fact —and I expressed
it openly as one of the causes of Australian dissatisfaction
with England, that we send to these splendid colonies
our most dangerous incapables. I denounce that as a
hitter injustice to the colonies. I met there 20 or 80
hopeless blackguards whom I had known over here
[What a lot of blackguards Christie knows !—“ tell me
your company,” &c.— Ed. B.] - Scotchmen and English-
men sent out by their parents simply because they were
wastrels.
The tiling Murray forgot to mention is that Eng-
land, on top of her other outrages, wan|s to know
why Australia isn’t grateful for this collection of
waste matter. When the Englishman starts out on
a philanthropic mission he always has one idea—
to send the poor, the crippled, the imbecile,
drunken, diseased and incurably vicious to some
other country, and leave them there. And then,
having thrown his dead dog over the wall into the
next man’s garden, and hit the owner of the
garden in the eye with the offensive matter, he is
quite surprised that the recipient of the beastly
thing doesn’t sing a hymn of praise and thanks-
giving.
The Foreign-traders of Sydney are hiring
canvassers to obtain signatures to a petition
praying the House of Aged Fossils to reject the
Customs Duties Bill and thus set up its feeble
old bones as a barrier against the expressed reso-
lution of the nation. Every house in Sydney is
to be visited, and in the gentle language of the
Telegraph, “ women as well as men shall have
an opportunity of recording their protest against
the new tariff.” They shall—in fact, most of the
signatures will be those of women. The average
woman never reads a petition, and will sign
almost anything, and she will depend entirely on
the explanations of the canvasser, who will tell
her that kid gloves are fivepence a pair dearer
through the duty. And then, in a very short
while, the Telegraph will speak about this docu-
ment as the emphatic protest of the great mass of
the “ electors of Sydney,” and will entirely forget
the fact that only one-sixth of the signatures were
electors and the rest were women, children, and
assorted infants.
Premier Griffith, of Queensland, has issued
a manifesto announcing his conversion to the
cause of nigger-labour, and the re-opening of the
kanaka slave traffic. Griffith rose to power first
on the cry of Australia for the white Australians.
He groaned for years and years about the suffer-
ings of the coloured serf on the northern planta-
tions ; he even shed a tear over him, and there
used to be a lump in his throat and crape on his
voice when lie talked about the burdened nigger.
His Government hounded the “Hopeful” prisoners
to a living death as a burnt-offering on the altar
of the ill-used Polynesian, and relentles.-h pi.- *ed
a miserably shady case against Sorenson, and con.
victed him on evidence that was too unspeakably
thin and suspicious to hold good against a dog-
all as a proof that they were solid on the white-
labour question. It was the only political prin.
ciple Griffith ever had —if he ever really
had it and now that it is gone he has
no political principle at all. In his new
capacity as an advocate of black-labour he is much
more eloquent than he ever was as an advocate for
white-labour. Whether he has a genuine belief
in either policy is doubtful —in fact, it is doubtful
if Griffith has a genuine belief or any other kind
of a belief in anything. But at present he holds
a brief for the big sugar-planter, and the labour-
trader, and the fatted syndicate, and he sheds the
same old tear over them as he used to shed over
the miserable Kanaka who was kidnapped from his
South Sea home under the nose of a drunken
“ Government agent,” sent out to see fair play on
board a “ blackbirding ” labour schooner. Griffith
has only one tear, and it is a glass imitation article
which he has used over and over again all through
his career. When he brings it out and fastens it
on his open countenance, everybody knows that
there is pathos coming, and that Sir Samuel has a
big brief in his pocket.
An obliging Bananalander in a far-out
township sends us five sides of news. Essence;
Two drunken local undertakers, performing the
last rites over a deceased publican, got the grave
made too small, and to make things level sawed
off half of the deceased boniface’s legs : likewise
the local doctor has a three-years’grog-prohibition
order out against him : the only sober local under-
taker is a Chinaman, the postmaster is mad, the
storekeeper and saddler are always in the horrors
and (ghastly climax !) the blacksmith is a Scotch-
man. Thanks.
“Any man who has not sufficient fore-
sight to provide for the future is an incubus
upon the State, and should be allowed to die,”
says politician - lawyer Crick, as reported in
Sydney Evening News. Good. Nearly all our
really brightest men die in the gutter, absolutely
or comparatively. Our country has not produced
many men who have said or written anything
worth remembering, but some of our geniuses,
such as they were, left not even a pair of socks’
and some were buried amongst murderers, and
some shot themselves because of over-Jew bills.
None of them provided for the future. It is the
dogs and wolves chiefly who perform this func-
tion. These snatch and bnry a bone, often with
the life-blood of the community scarcely dry upon
it—then they get an “ extra vote for thrift ” from
the “National” Association —they dig up their
plant by night and it provides for their future,
even when it reeks with corruption. Let us howl
a panegyric for these provident hoarders of bones.
THE NATIONAL ASS.’S NEW PLATFORM.
One vote for Manhood and one vote for Thrift,
None to the wretch who is poor and adrift,
One for the toiler hard striving to live,
Two for the lucky rich loafer they’d give,
For Thrift in the sight of the National Ass.
Is proved in the sacred possession of “brass ;”
Possession by chance, or by striving alway
To grab what you can and give little away ;
To screw, and to pinch, and to chisel your neighbour,
To feed a tat purse by the starving of labour.
Ye gods! we know thousands who work on this plan,
who'd
Win votes for their Thrift by the loss of their Manhood,
Rescued from Misery.
John L. Aubrey, Esq., Sofala, N.S.W., kindly
furnishes the following testimony “In January,
1879 I was master of a provisional school in tho
western district, and I married a delicate young
lady, and was shortly afterwards appointed
teacher under the Board of Education. Our first
child was born in September 18S0, after which my
wife never seemed to regain her usual strength.
In May, 1881, she was suddenly taken very ill;
the family doctor was sent for and was greatly
puzzled ; he, however, prescribed and the patient
Partly recovered. About twelve months later
she was very much troubled with a painful
throbbing movement in the abdomen, especially
when lying down, loss of appetite, and what food
was taken afforded no nourishment. Another
doctor was called in, who sounded and prescribed,
but to no purpose. We left the neighbourhood
and went to Lithgow Valley, hoping to effect an
improvement, but without result. Another doctor
did all he could and decided that the case was one
of enlargement of the liver, and this proved to be
the case, as the side sometimes swelled so large as
to prevent dressing; so matters progressed and
got from bad to worse. Numerous doctors con-
tinued to do but little good and that only
temporary, but all agreed that the svstem was
generally weak and wanted building up; and the
liver would sometimes swell and burst off button
after button off her ulster while standing in a store.
For the last two years the trouble has been com-
plicated with a most distressing rushing noise in
the head, so violent as to render it impossible to
lie in bed at night, with violent attacks of windy
colic, causing complete prostration, and excoria-
tion of the throat. Eventually, after further
sufferings, I read of Clements Tonic and its
wonderful effects, and sent for a bottle. The
first dose was too strong for the debilitated
stomach. I diluted it with water and gradually
increased it as the stomach could bear it. Mrs.
Aubrey has now taken three bottles and the effect
is mo.t marvellous; the swelling has entirely
disappeared, and the waist is reduced by seven
inches. The eyesight is renewed and strengthened,
the appetite returned, the noise in the head
diminished, and, to use ray wife’s own words,
‘Language utterly fails to describe the relief and
benefit she has experienced by the use of only
three bottles of Clements Tonic.’ 1 consider
Clements Tonic the most palatable and effective
medicine I have ever used in cither England,
India, Africa, or Australia.— John E. Aubrey.”

The latest story about a certain eccentric
Australian police magistrate. His liver was ex-
ceptionally troublesome that day, and when he
looked up at the gallery, which was well filled, as
usual, he got worse. He said: “Clear that
gallery. Just look at the horrible faces on some
of those lazy loafers. I don’t believe there are
faces as ugly as some of those in hell! ” .
The cherubic-looking editor of a Mel-
bourne paper is a dude, and a frugal dude at that.
He used to get his clothes made “at Home” and
consigned to a Sydney friend, so saving the duty.
The last consignment to hand, however, arrived
at Sydney just after the new tariff came into
force, and just now there is a Melbourne editor
walking round with stylish but frayed pants,
praying for a speedy triumph of Cornstalk Free-
trade.

despise a knighthood,” and that “Higinbotham,
if ever offered it, refused it because he didn’t think
that an handle would be an ornament to his
plebeian name.” Our correspondent carefully
avoids giving his own name, which is probably
Hogchops. A name doesn’t make a man an aris-
tocrat, but the man may make his name noble.
Higinbotham has done that.

Dr. Ulrich Hiibbe, who recently died in
Adelaide, was the draftsman of R. R. Torrens’
famous Real Property Act. Hiibbe got the
idea in Germany, and nearly the whole measure
was the work of his brain. He brought from the
Fatherland the idea that mortgages should not
change the freehold, but should be charges in
priority. The Doctor always thought himself
badly treated by his adopted country, and very
justly he considered his services should have been
amply rewarded.
It is related of Mr. Jenks, late Law Pro-
fessor at Melbourne University (who has just left
for England), when he got married a year or two
ago, that on the day the vessel bearing his “ to
be ” arrived in port, he went to the University in
the morning and conducted a class ; at mid-day
he hastened to the pier, brought away from the
vessel his bride, escorted her to a church and
married her, then returned to the University again
and delivered a profound lecture upon Jurispru-
dence. How’s that for a day’s work ?
The late Dr. Perry, once Bishop of Mel-
bourne, loft behind him £31,000. Cardinal
Manning died worth only £lOO, but in fairness to
Perry it should be remembered that married
clergymen of Protestant churches have widows
and families to provide for, whereas the Roman
priests are celibate. However, no clergyman who
would do justice to the Christian religion can
afford to die worth more than his books and fur-
niture and a life-assurance for the benefit of his
immediate heirs.
Fraser Smith, the Hongkong editor—he
was in Sydney a few months ago —has practically
been ruined by the little Crown-colony clique,
against whose aggression he has fought so long
and so manfully, and his friends are raising a
fund to free him from money trouble. The “ set”
made against him reminds one of the experiences
of Edward Smith Hall and other men who, in
early Australia, fought; for public right as against
Imperial official privilege. Through official
machinations Fraser Smith was denied the use of
every local hall, and had to hold an open-air
meeting.
At an Otago election meeting John Lar
nach was asked his opinion of Fergus, his (Lar
nach’s) successor as Minister of Mines. The chair-
man objected to the question as improper. “ I
have no objection to it,” suavely put in Larnach ;
“I consider Mr. Fergus a very handsome gentle-
man with a very large beard.” Another story of
Larnach : Editor Twopenny, of Otago Daily
Times, known throughout Alaoriland as a breeder
of dogs, threatened to oppose Larnach for the
Peninsula seat. At a meeting the sitting member
referred to his opponent thus: “The gentleman
calls himself a leader of public opinion. Well, all
I have ever known him to lead is a fox-terrier
stallion round this district, for pecuniary pur-
poses. ”
The German princes of Pless, one of
whom has just wedded Miss Cornwallis West, are
a curious lot. They are all named John Henry,
and they are all numbered after the fashion of
monarchs, or convicts, or registered dogs, or stud
bulls; and as the numbers take in not only the
direct line of princes but all their cousins and
second cousins, and their twenty-third nephews,
and their one-hundred-and-seventy-ninth uncles,
it has come to pass at last that nearly all the in-
habitants in their locality are John Henrys with
a number. When the numbers go up to a certain
point—a hundred or so—they start afresh ;by the
time it reaches John Henry lxxxxix the thing is
pretty monotonous, and the people want some
variety. (N.B.—This paragraph is written by
Thomas lxiii.)
Many prominent Maoriland barristers are
self-made men, sprung direct from the plebs.
Clever Theo. Cooper, possessor of the finest prac-
tice, sold newspapers in the streets of Auckland,
and afterwards graduated in the machine-room of
an Auckland morning paper; so also did his
brothers “Ted” and William, both successful
forensically. W. J. Napier, who, single-handed,
beat M'Arthur and Co. in the great Samoan land-
case, is the son of a shoeblack, and fifteen years
ago was a boy in a tobacconist’s shop. Hon. Joe
Tole, an ex-Minister of Justice, the son of a com-
mon soldier (named O’Toole), and Thomas Catter
are sons of the proletariat now enjoying big
practices at the Bar. Sir Robert Stout himself is
entirely self-taught, and is the son of a very
“ small ” Shetland Island shopkeeper.
A n escapade of Richard Egan Lee, author
of the now celebrated “W. G. Grace letter.” We
change the names, that is all—he himself told the
story to the present writer. He arrived at Cow-
town just exactly in the nick of time —said the
proprietor of the local Observer. This potentate
had been slangwhanged by a woman, call her
Mrs. Bullfinch, who edited the local paper in a
neighbouring town. “Give it her hot,’’said he,
and we will call him Sugars. So, Dick “did her
up ” in one of his choicest W. G. Grace styles,
beginning, “ When a female baboon squats in a
gutter, and slings mud at respectable journalists,
it is time to protest.” This duly appeared, say,
on Thursday. On Saturday, after receiving his
wages, Dick, eating pears in the orchard at the
back of the newspaper printing-office, heard
a terrific din, as if everything was being smashed
in the place. He rightly divined that it was Mrs.
Bullfinch’s two brothers chasing Sugars round
with sticks, and so he scooted right off out of the
town.
The father of a certain brilliant, but now
departed, N.S.W. barrister, was a worthy old
Northern dairyman, who sacrificed much to
obtain for his son a liberal education and went
wild with delight when young got his first
brief from a friendly solicitor. The old man
attended to witness his son’s first appearance in
horsehair, and invited a number of his friends and
as many of the unoccupied jurymen as he could
find to join him in celebrating the occasion.
After had made one of those impassioned
appeals to the twelve which the young Irish-
Australian so greatly affects, the jury retired, and
his father hastened down to G ’s Hotel, close
to the Court-house, to tell his friends all about it.
“ Gintlemin,” said the old man, “’twas the
proudest moment of me life, and I’m well repaid
for all me expinses an Janny’s edjucation. For
what did I see in the Coort above? There was
the Joodge wrangling wid the barristhers, and the
barristhers wrangling wid the lawyers, and, by
G , gintlemin, there was my Janny wrangling
wid the whole lot o’ them.”
The citizens of Glasgow, it is cabled, are
promoting subscriptions for the persecuted
Russian Jews. An Irish blaggard here remarks
that “ a fellow feeling makes us wondrous kind.”
When the N.S.W. Harbors and Rivers
Department called for tenders for 60 tracings of
machinery for the new Defence Dept., the tenders
ranged from £2B to £l5O, and most of tht
tenderers were not draftsmen at all, but clerks,
artisans, &c. Nearly all the draftsmen and archi-
tects who arrived recently from Melbourne and
Maoriland, are returning owing to the dearth of
work.
Resolution passed by the Pahiatua (M.L.)
county council:—
The public, whenever each or any one of them And a
short cut from anywhere to anywhere else, are to immedi-
ately publicly notify it for the benefit of anyand all those
whom it may concern, and that a copy of this resolution
be forwarded to the Makuri township.
The Bulletin rises to express its utter
disbelief in the existence of any “Dora Dora
blacks.” It is tired of reading in the daily papers
the recital of the fabulous achievements of
“ Sarjint O’Smiley, capably assisted by Saynyer
Constable O’Blatherty and Constable Bannigan,”
who “thracked ” the desperadoes amid the glow-
ing haze of a tropical day, and the murky
mysteries of an insect-governed night, for lofij
Irish miles, and who, at the very moment when
men and horses fell into dead faints on a clump of
prickly-pears, could just see the two niggers
sitting straddle-legged on the edge of the horizon.
Assorting and analysing all the alleged circum-
stances connected with thehistory of these mythical
outlaws, The Bulletin asserts its suspicion that
they were deliberately invented in order to pro-
vide pleasant picnics and extra-duty allowances
for mouldering policemen. It refuses to believe
that there are any Dora Dora blacks whatever,
and, if the allegations concerning them contain
any grain of truth, it will probably eventually be
discovered that they are policemen in disguise,
who have taken to the road, and are secretly
supplied with provisions by their comrades in the
force. And in further support of this theory it
may be pointed out that nobody has actually seen
any blacks from the very beginning of the scare
until now, and the only time that any man
thought he saw one and fired at him he shot a
respectable settler in the abdomen, and, so far as
.any evidence went, there wasn’t a real Dora Dora
black within a hundred miles.

JUST OUT.
LIFE OF C. SB. PARNELL,
By T. P. O’OONNOII, M.P.
Price (post jree), Is. 2d.
“Mr. O’Connor has certainly surpassed himself in the
writing of this intensely interesting work. ... No
newspaper notice, however full, can give any adequate
impression of the career as is here depicted of the great
Irishman who has gone. Mr. O’Connor’s book itself should
be in the hands of everyone who is interested in the
brilliant and stormy career which is ended for ever. . .
A delightful, graphic and biographical sketch of one of
(lie most remarkable figures of the century.”—Dublin
Freeman's Journal.
F inn Bros. & Co.,
586 George Street, SYDNEY.
Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., St.
James Chambers, King-street, Sydney
Society
A big Sydney bank, which holds the bulk
of the securities taken by Mr. Abraham Friedman,
the busted money-lender, from his “clients,” has
commenced legal proceedings in a wholesale fashion
against Friedman’s debtors. As it is well-known
that politicians formed a considerable portion of
Friedman’s clientele, it is more than probable that
Abraham’s collapse will cause some M.P.’s who
did business with him to face their constituents
ere long.
* * *
Dailey once prophesied that Parkes would
yet rule over “a nation of creditors.” If the
Dibbs Government follow their present track,
they will yet reign over a nation of inspectors.
The latest inspector is Captain Le Patourel, late
of the N.S.W. Artillery, who is to “inspect the
N.S.W. rifle ranges” in return for “ .£2OO a year
and travelling allowances.” When we become
old and hoary, may Heaven send us as soft an
official bed whereon to lay our aching bones !
• * *
Pastor Soltau, of Tasmania, who claims
that it was he who fetched along the Launceston
earthquake by prayer as a punishment for the
local sinners, will presently have reason to wish
he had never been born. A Melbourne syndicate
owning property on the island has served him with
a writ for .£250,000 damages for shook, malicious
concussion, and wilful destruction to their pro-
perty. We can hear with the capacious ear of
prophecy the Pastor under examination in the
witness-box. “ Did you not state that you fetched
along that earthquake by prayer.” “I did.”
“ You are aware, no doubt, that one old woman
died of fright; do you admit murdering that old
woman?’ “I—ahem—that is a matter which
rests entirely with the Lord. I am only a
Numble Instrument.” “ You are also aware that
much damage was done to property. Do you
admit causing that damage?” “I—ahem —I
protest against this worldly view of the matter. I
am only a vessel in the hands of Providence?”
“ Are you willing to pay up the damage? ” “No.”
“ Then you don’t admit that you caused the
injuries and killed the old woman?” “No.”
“ Then you didn’t bring about the earthquake ? ”
“No, I didn’t.” “Then when you said you
caused it by prayer the statement wasn’t true ? ”
“No.” “ What do you call a man who makes a
statement that isn’t true ? ” Dead silence and a
long and horrible pause.
* • *
Madame Carandini was “farewelled” in a
very neat and kindly way at Allan’s, the afternoon
previous to the veteran nightingale’s final de-
parture from Melbourne. A little group of men
who knew her when she was from 20 to 40 years
younger handed over (per Mr. Fred Tate) a purse
containing 130 sovereigns, and anon the secretary
of Madame’s concert at the Town Hall came
forward with a cheque for ,£2OO, under the weight
of all which tangible expression of good-will the
lady fairly broke down. It is not often that the
musical world of Melbourne comes out so freely
with its money, but, then, in this instance the
good cause happened to be an especially handsome
and charming woman.
v * * •
A late “popular” Australian Governor,
who used to be hand-in-glove with everybody
who could cheer him, was ineffably stingy in his
own establishment. “ Meat once a day,” was his
healthy, economical rule as regarded his menials.
At the expiration of his term, not One of his ser-
vants would return to Hingland with him ; he
never shook hands, nor even bleated a fatherly
farewell to any of his commoner old retainers, and
as to the disbursement of parting remembrances—
no fear.
* # #
If the Earl of Glasgow had a mania for
the turf, like his crotchetty old predecessor in the
title (who bred Musket’s sire), one could under-
stand him being sent out to bid for the sporting
vote and interest of Maoriland. The incoming
Gov., however, is alleged to be a dreary nonentity
and a pious infliction of the most respectable
character. He hates racing, and is altogether a
dry-hash individual in his 00th year. Evidently
Knutsford considers that anything will do for the
beastly Kawlinies (especially Maoriland) nowa-
days, provided that it carries a Scotch title.
Kintore, Hopetoun, Hamilton, Glasgow! The
Duke of Edinburgh will soon be forwarded as
King of Australasia if we don’t take care.
* * *
A Melbourne suburban Mayor—a butcher
generously announced that on a certain night
he would distribute free meat to the unemployed.
He did so—gave away at least a ton. Yery
philanthropic-looking, but—he had appropriated
part of his mayoral banquet-allowance to provide
the meat. Having supplied that article himself
he did a good stroke of business, beside posing as
a public benefactor and helping along his parlia-
mentary candidature. That, and the fact that, as
the allowance came from the ratepayers, the
starving men probably paid for the meat them-
selves after all, is one .aspect of the case. The
other is that, had the Mayor been like aldermen
in general, he would have spent the money in
greasing the fat pigs at the Town Hall.

Dear Bulletin, —In last issue you asK
why a dairyman troubled with a dishonest driver
can’t Chubb-lock his milk-cans ? To what pur-
pose, O ! usually sapient journal ? The same
spout that serves for pouring out the cow-juice is
always open to receive tribute from the pump.—
Yours obediently. One Who Has Been There.
(Vain spouter, milk-cans are emptied by taps.—
Ed. B.]
» # »
One of the most skilful Melbourne inter-
viewers has just come off best in a bout with a
slippery politician. This individual played the
bplly, and wanted to make out that he had been
misreported. In this case the editor, contrary
to the usual practice, stuck to his reporter, and
the M.L. A. was routed. Yet he has hectored
other papers into grilling their interviewers. The
old Tory newspaper proprietors have a most
absurd theory that an interviewer must shorthand
it. Experience tells you that a man will never
talk under such circumstances, unless he be a
self-seeking sixth-rate prig—who’ll write his own
interview,
* # #
A correspondent from China writes in a
Melbourne daily last week, that the missionaries
live there like fighting cocks. In a river steamer,
he travelled with nuns, who wouldn’t look at him,
but each put away at dinner a couple of glasses of
sherry and half a tumbler of claret; nor are the
Protestant missionaries behind in luxurious living.
OUT OF THE QUESTION.
YOUNG MASHER (proceeding to tell old swell a story) : “ The other night a girl who’s a
friend of mine— I mean a friend of my wife-”
OLD SWELL ( impressively) : “ My dear young man, no girl who is a friend of your’s
can possibly be a friend of your wife.”
In a gidya clump, about four chains from
the beaten track, six miles from Widgcegoara
bead-station, off the main road to Cunnamulla
(Q.), the traveller will, a correspondent tells us,
come upon a heap of white bones, an old billy-can,
a pannikin, a perished waterbag, heavy hobnailed
blucher boots, rotted shreds of clothing, swag-
straps, &c. —a collection of things eloquent of the
hard death of one out of many hundreds who
perish by thirst in the Australian bush. These
bones belong to one who so died in the drought of
’BB-9. An enquiry was duly held and orders were
issued for burial. But the dingoes sharpened
their teeth on the bones, the crows feasted on the
snn-carrioned flesh, and the remnants bleach
openly, a testimony to official laisser faire. It is
wondered if burial vouchers ever passed through
the departments on this count.
* * *
Solicitor Trickett, of Sydney, confesses
once to have been in a tight place. He had drawn
the will of old Mrs. Fox, who owned a city pro-
perty in King-street, adjoining the Daily Tele-
graph office. On the old lady’s death he
assembled the people interested or alleged to be
interested in the old lady’s affairs and read the
will. Amongst the expectant legatees was an old
woman who had daily waited upon the deceased
“just to take a drop o’ tay or sich like.” This
lady was an expectant to the extent at least of
half the property, but item after item was read
and yet her name did not appear. Mr. Trickett
finished reading, and the old lady’s wrath knew
no bounds. She cursed the dead, and, had the
“casket” been at hand, she declared she would
have opened it and “ spat ” upon the corpse. She
seemed to think the lawyer her mortal enemy, and
had he not departed, would have “gone for him.”
Mrs. Fox left most of her money to “St. Mary’s.”

M. L. and G. Co. be convicted, depositors and

pany-promoters will start for quod via Darling-
hurst dock.
* * *
The Melbourne Journalists’ Union deletes
the clause for admission of proprietors, as recom-
mended in the painstaking letter they received
from Chief Justice Higinbotham. It would look
odd if the newspaper proprietors of any city saw
their way to combine.
* * *
Wig and gown are doomed in Melbourne.
The attorneys have carried their point, and need
not don the stuff and horsehair unless they like.
Very soon it will disappear, in the convenient
summer time, oven off the Bench.
A story (told by a Queensland paper,
•which ought to be true. There had been a vice-
regal gathering, and when it was over a fan was
found among the debris. The A.D.C. thought it
belonged to a certain Miss Smith, and wrote to
her about it, signing himself, “ Yours, Binning.
after the accustomed style of the British lord.
The answer he received paralysed him so much
that his eye-glass got fixed in his eye for 40 days,
and had to be removed at last by a surgeon. It
read : “ Dear Binning,—The fan doesn’t belong to
me.—Yours, Smith.”

The share-market value of the Melbourne
cable tramways has fallen seven millions sterling
within the last 12 months.
* # #
An English family in Melbourne advertises
thusly :
"LTOUSEKEEPER for small English family in plea-
Ai sant suburb; will be required to cook, wash,
and generally look after house. A lady will be
treated quite as one of the family. References and
wages required.
That’s what will happen to the “ lady ” when she
is treated as one of the family. What would
happen to her if she was treated as a stranger ?

During the recent Broken Hill water-
famine a South Australian firm of merchants sent
their representative at the “ Hill ” two tanks of
water as a New Year’s gift.

It is well known that should the Y.D.L.
liquidators make a call upon the shareholders
blackest ruin will stare numberless Tasmanian
families in the face. Fortunately it now seems
certain that the liquidation will be effected with-
out such a call.

The advances made to a director and the
manager of the Bank of Y.D.L. amounted to one-
third of the paid-up capital of the bank !
* * #
At Bridgend (Eng.), the other day, Judge
Williams had an unfinished case on hand at the
time when he had to leave by train. He took
the remaining witness and the barristers into the
carriage with him, heard the rest of the evidence
and counsels’ addresses en route, and gave his
judgment in the railway-station at the end of the
journey. We don’t do things that way in Aus-
tralia. A jury case was being tried in the N.S.W.
Supreme Court on the last day of the December
sittings, and as it was not finished by four o’clock
sharp, Judge Innes adjourned it till next March.
* # #
The awful fire at the Hotel Royal, [New
York, is a warning over the Sydney and Mel-
bourne sky-palaces. The sensation is bound to
come, and it is a toss-up at which of the “ Coffee
Pallises. ” The lodger jumps out of an eight-storey
window, and is cut to ribbons on the telegraph-
wires. In Melbourne they have buildings up to
twelve-storeys. “My turn next,” says the cock-
lofter.
PADDY MAGEE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
What are you doing now, Paddy Magee ?
Grafting, or spelling now, Paddy Magee?
Breaking, or branding?
Or overlanding,
Out on the sand ridges, Paddy Magee?
Is your mouth parched, from an all-night spree ?
Taking a pick-me-up, Paddy Magee?
Cocktail —or simple soda and h. ?
Which is the “ antidote,” Paddy Magee ?
Still “shook” on some beautiful, blushing she?
Girl in the Bogan side, Paddy Magee ?
A hack providing
For moonlight riding,
Side-saddle foolery, Paddy Magee?
Up on the station—or in the town—
Or on the Warrego, droving down,
Whatever you're doing—wherever you be !
“ There’s lashin'so’ luck to ye !” Paddv Magee !

I‘or poor and humble, for rich and great:
Fin de sifecle Equity,
Equity up to date !
Ibsen heroines, shamelessly frail,
“ Inherent taints ” through a sickly tale ;
Or plays in a backwoods dialect,
With plenty of realistic effect,
With horses galloping up the straight,
Gunpowder, tanks, and a five-barred gate:
The Fin de si bole Drama,
The Drama up to date !
Dishonest jockeys, who ride to lose,
Bookmakers standing them tips and booze,
To let some unbacked outsider in,
And “pull ” the horse that could fairly win,
In such a way that they seem to “ wait”
And “ make their effort ” a trifle late :
Fin de sihole Horse-racing,
Horse-racing up to date !
Parliament used as a stepping-stone
To nobody's benefit, but his own,
By every member—a frantic race
lor snug appointments and power and place ;
Special Committees-to gorge and fete ;
Personal wrangles, misnamed debate;
Ein de sibcle Polities,
Politics up to date !
The realistic in prose and verse
(“ Most of it bad, and some of it worse 1”)
Or mystic stories some ghostly hand
Might well have penned in Mahatma land ;
Papers that slavishly adulate
Fossil abuses they ought to slate ;
Fin de siecle Literature,
Literature up to date !
Melbourne, 11/2/92.

The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*
The Euoalypte Lozenges for colds, made by
Colemane and Sons, are unsurpassed.

The mau who makes a pile by his own
efforts has to be dreadfully mean to do it and his
eff nness sticks to him for ever afterwards. Any
"nt who happens to drop into a George-street
(Svdney) restaurant, and watch a certain enonn-
mislv rich man who lunches there every day, will
presently realise this. It is an eighteenpenny
P Sshment, and he goes there partly as a
e r ifice to appearances, and partly because he
sao ” h the Premises ; but he would much rather
patronise the sixpenny place down at the Chinese
Therefore he tries to get level by eating
thing on the table, and there is a grieved
expression^ on his countenance when he has to go
way and leave something behind him. He con-
fines all the sauce in the bottle, and all the green
Sin the dish, regularly. He empties the little
teapot that is brought to him, and then he shakes
it and then he takes off the lid and looks in to
make quite sure that no fluid is left concealed
among the leaves. He uses all the sugar in the
basin and demolishes the butter, and generally
he gets right through the bread. Then he takes
‘the milk-jug, glares into it with one eye, and
solemnly drinks up the contents. But the thing
that obviously preys on his mind is the cruet. He
~a n’ t eat all the mustard, yet it rends his vitals to
leave it behind, and he regards it with a wistful
expression which seems to imply that his great
soul is struggling with a sense of defeat. Cast ot
all he gathers up his ticket, heaves a heart-broken
sigh, and goes away.
# * *
That Australian “Silver Queen” is so-
journing in Cheltenham, Eng., where the presence
of so eligible a widow has created quite a flutter
of excitement. Of course her wealth has been
magnified tenfold !
* * *
You may contradict, writes our London
correspondent, the absurd story that Gin———
has in his office in London a door leading to his
room on which is inscribed. “Private entrance
for ladies only.”
The Melbourne
papers tell a story of a
Carlton dentist who was
lately sued for mainten-
ance’. He was not long
married, and when the.
first ardour of his affec-
tion cooled down be con-
fiscated the presents he
had given his wife, and
even boiled down her
wedding - ring to make
settings for false teeth.
Truly, it is sharper than
a serpent’s tooth to be
married to an unfeeling
dentist.
# * *
Jemmy Grimes,
whilom Toowoomba, (tj.),
auctioneer, is now
living in Norfolk, near
Mauiidsley, where he lias
leased a nice property
and is playing the
country gentleman with
considerable success. Of
course be is reputed to
be enormously rich, and
this counts for a good
deal in England. His
wife is a clever woman,
and writes short stories
for magazines. She is
engaged on a new novel,
dealing, of course, with
life in Australia. —“ Rolf
Boldrewood ” has made
that style of literature
cfuite the fashion. She
intends to introduce a
“corroboree” set of
music, which is probably
the first time such a
Wagnerian feat lias ever
been attempted.
* * *
A Melbourne State
school-mistress thumped
a little girl the other day
until the child ><is deaf
and sick. Asapient Board
of Advice thereupon
held a special meeting,
shrieked loudly against
such disobedience of the
regulations concerning
boxing on the ears, “ad-
vised ” the gentle teacher
not to do it again, and
then decided that the
case was not one to
trouble the department
with. Moral; Always be born with a relative
among your bosses.

* # *
Another church organist has come to grief,
—this time embezzlement and suicide —in Banana-
land. Church choirs seem wedded to crime. And
when in gaol, how sweetly did that forger play
the organ !
# * •
It has been noted that the most violent
advocates of total abstinence in N.S. Wales are
hopeless slaves of their nipes. Similarly, the chief
leader of the liquorphobia movement in Brisbane,
“ Blue Ribbon ” Clark, is a bondsman to nicotine.
He has “ sworn off ” smoking several times to the
writer’s knowledge, hurled away his pipe with a
pious anathema and a good resolution, given away
his tobacco and pouch, and —been seen strutting
down Queen-street next day with a seven-inch
cigar in his mouth. Sydney’s own Niman Mel-
ville, too, is afflicted with smokomania and is
understood to go to bed with his pipe in his
mouth. Were it not against the rules_ of the
House, he’d often smoke out the Committee of
the Whole when hon. members are stonewalling.
So it is with most of the chief anti-alcoholic
bigots. Smoking is their darling sin. But it is
not their only darling sin, mind you.

A VERBATIM REPORT.
NEWSPAPER BOSS: “ Why haven’t you left Mrs. Batcher’s paper for a whole week? ”
RUNNER: “ Yes, I did.”
NEWSPAPER BOSS: “But she says not.”
RUNNER: “ She’s an old, jumped-up liar.”
NEWSPAPER BOSS: “Well, she hasn’t got’em.” , ,
RUNNER: “ No, cos her goat eats ’em reglar soon’s I throw ’em over on the v'randy.
NRESPAPER ROSS: “Why don’t you chase the goat off?”
RUNNER: “ Yus ! An’ have the old Snapper bring a haction agen me for chucking things at her goat.? Not me!"
Miss Laura Fowler, who is just appointed to-
Adelaide Children’s Hospital, is the daughter of
a local merchant, and obtained high honours at
Adelaide University.
* * *
Some years ago a young and exceedingly
rich Australian lawyer got badly smitten by a
beautiful actress. He constantly attended the
theatre where she appeared, and thus became
mixed up with theatrical people generally. Ihe
young actress left the stage and married -not the
lawyer, but quite another fellow, and the lawyer
now practically “bosses” the theatre where his
idol used to play. There is a big moral in this.
* * •
Broken is the golden bowl. The new
Mayor of North Sydney and the six reporters who
attend the meeting of the Council ff Eighteen
never speak as they pass by. When chance placed
him in the chair the other night, his morose Wor-
ship thanked the aldermen for the honour “ con-
ferred,” and said, “if they followed him to the
nearest puli, he would do the needful. Ihe press
noticed the pointed way in which it was ignored,
and remained glued to its seat. 1 hen a resolu-
tion was unanimously passed to adjourn to the
other snake-juice emporium and put » »ob m,
&c., not for the purpose of publication, but as a
matter of good faith.
Upon the corpse of W. G. Long, a recently
disrated Brisbane (Q.) solicitor, was found a pawn-
ticket, with the endorsement (after his wife’s
address)—“ Nothing to do, same to eat, but plenty
to drink.” There was also a declaration, signed
by an unknown person and witnessed by Long—
“ J limit myself to one bottle of beer each day,
and that such shall be brought to me, so that I
shall not be in any way required to enter a hotel.”
Pathetic resolution !
* * *
Colonel Tom Price’s Lay-’em-outs appear
in an absolutely barmaid-smashing new uniform.
Tom has been among the Italian Bersaglien, and
caps the Confederate Butternut uniform with a
handful of black cock’s feathers in the turned-np
scolloped sombrero; besides a lot of bloody (look-
ing) braid meandering over the chest, and a gay
belt of Lord Sheffield’s resplendent colours. It’s
immense.
# * *
About eight years ago a new court-house was-
built at Deniliquin, N.S. W., at a cost of .£15,000.
Owing to the interior being improperly fitted up,
the court has never been used, and business is
carried on in the old court-house, which is good
enough for all practical purposes, seeing that the
Supreme Court only sits twice a year. Mean-
while, ,£15,000 worth of taxpayers’ property is
crumbling away.
# * *
Efforts are being made to bring out a
Sunday paper in Brisbane.
* • •
A N.S.W. Supreme Courc bailiff is said to
be missing from a country town, as also certain
big sums of current coin.
* # *
The Sydney “pushes” are becoming as
formidable as the Italian secret societies. At the
inquest on the Lower George-street murder case,
witness Finnessey was clearly the subject of
intimidation. Many people affirm that the Surry
Hills murderer escapes justice through the “ push”
having systematically intimidated the few who
“know.” There are now to the “credit” of
alleged larrikins in Sydney no less than three un-
avenged corpses.

* * *
Junctions, six shillings ! Ye gods !
* * *
The whereabouts of Speight, Chief Com-
missioner of Railways, has been the Melbourne
mystery of these latter days. Two Saturdays ago
that inspired failure went up-country, or down the
bay, or somewhere, and during an awful period of
suspense a great multitude of Victorian engines
were allowed to go puffing to and fro like sheep
without a shepherd. The Chief Commissioner, in
short, was lost to everything save his salary, which
stuck to him as usual. He left no postal address
for the benefit of the reporters, and no sort of clue
that could be followed up by the black-trackers
with any hope of success. An unaccountable
absence of Speight at such a dull time is not to be
borne in silence. He is the mainspring of political
oratory in Victoria, so to speak, and the sole sup-
port of Melbourne’s alleged “ comic” paper. Take
Speight out of public life and the Cabbage Garden
becomes a mere wilderness with nothing but a bad
smell to fall back upon. Under the circumstances
The Bulletin calls upon a troubadour to make
enquiries on this subject at the fountain-head i
Princess Beatrice —are you listening,
ladies? —was announced to be “ convalescent ’■
eight dajs after the birth of her last baby.
# * *
Those who ask, “Are the Sydney Jews
sweaters?” are certainly not humourists. Jews
don’t work hard enough to sweat.
* * *
A Melbourne weekly paper publishes every
week a ghastly caricature of lovely woman, and
invariably refers to it as the “ pretty portrait ” of
some prominent actress. Each one. of the series
might pass muster as an imaginative picture of
Jezebel before she had been painted up for the
day.
OUR PATHS DIVERGE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Our paths diverge.” Six years have fled
Since first you spoke those words and turned
To leave the rival whom you spurned,
“ She loves you not,” you, sneering, said,
“Our paths diverge.”
Six years ago the world was fair,
An English landscape bathed in light
And summer beauty ; all more bright
For she, adored by both, was there
Six years ago.
Alas, too late, my love found voice,
You’d won her heart (she said) and hand,
And I, I left my native land, . „
Where Love had whispered once Rejoice,
Alas, too late.
I’ll take your hand ; I never thought
To see you dying in the south,
To see the blood-foam on your mouth—
Despite the agony you’ve wrought,
I'll take your hand.
I never thought that she could die,
That you would wander o’er my path ;
I’ve ne’er recalled you save in wrath,
Of her, except with longing, I
I never thought.
You’d speak again ? Your hand grows cold ;
The Morn is flushing all the grey ;
I scarce can hear what you would say ,
You smile, but not as once of old ;
You’d speak again ?
“ Our paths diverge.” Dear friend, ah no! >
Strength comes with Day. . . . My God, he s dead .
They two have met, and I must tread
A lonely world —-as long ago,
Our paths diverge 1
THE PHOENIX
TO THE GODDESS OE TRUTH.
Tell us, goddess, pure, undraped,
You who know our frail ways,
Whereunto had Speight escaped
When he fled the railways ?
Leave us not in fear and doubt ;
Say—what was the man about?
Was he roaming far and wide,
Or in calm seclusion.
With refreshment at his side,
Free from all intrusion ?
Was he romping in the hay.
Spite of lines that never pay ?
Was he picnicking with Zox,
Likewise Duncan Gillies,
Camping out in baokest blocks,
Boiling tea in billies?
Was it on a rural burst
That he started, Feb. the first?
Ne’er a farewell word he spake
Telling of his movements,
P’raps he went to test a brake,
One with new improvements ?
Was it biz. or pleasure led
Speight to seem the same as dead ?
Tell us, goddess white as snow
(Secret this between us),
Had a shaft from Cupid’s bow
Made him slave to Venus ?
Did he slip away to spoon
By the light of yonder moon ?
What became of potent Speight
Whilst they sought him vainly—
Did he go to bed at eight ?
Was it duty mainly
Took him from his office-chair
Far away to Lord knows where?
Goddess, in but virtue clad,
Queen of subjects all dead,
Tell us where that rorty lad
Hid his bouncing, bald head ?
Truth divine, this story tell
From the bottom of thy well.

the price of bread under the circumstances stated in the
paragraph thus; —Firstly, the increased local production
of wheat and flour consequent on the farmers having
secured the local market for themselves would ordinarily
ensure a full supply; secondly, when a ring of bakers
combined to raise the price of bread by £6 a ton, whereas
the duty charged on flour was ,t;X per ton only, other
bakers, seeing a big margin and a chance to cut
in, would attack them on their own ground.
Prices would then be reduced, fast enough.... ‘ A.
Kell’; It is much better, in a pecuniary sense, to

corrupt system. The man with social and business ties is
unfit to be a magistrate. No man can be a competent
judge in his own cause or in that of his friends. They
used to pack the benches in Sydney—pack them unblush-
mgly. They still pack them in the country districts. In
Melbourne they pack them all the time, and the only
letnedy is to altogether debar honorary beakdom from
sitting on the bench. (2.) If the Judge has, as would
appear to be the case, injured you by making remarks
based on a misconception, write him a letter explaining,
but lie careful to stick to the point. If you convince him
that he has acted unjustly, he will no doubt make public
reparation. (Many replies held over.)

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Potts Point mansion of a certain book-
maker has been burgled of much money and many choice
cigars, but the robber missed some valuable jewellery.
We are so sorry. Jewellery looks well on thieves, but not
upon bookmakers.
# # *
In George Darrell’s Adelaide tug-of-war, two
teams, representing Sweden and Irish sailors, started at
eight o’clock and pulled the whole evening—result, a
draw. George stopped this by limiting contests to
30min,
* * *
When a tug-of-war lasts two hours and a-half it
is as enjoyable as Bannerman’s batting.
• » »
N.S.W. Juniors, Driver and Camphin, played
remarkably good cricket for their innings of 71 each, and
the Britishers in the field looked as if they were
thoroughly sick of the contract.
* # #
It will set the minds of many people at rest, if
we state that the R. Driver who played so well in the
recent match—Sydney Juniors v. the Englishmen—is no
relative of old-time “Dicky” Driver. Camphin, who
shared the honours with Driver, is a son of the well-
known detective-inspector.
* * #
The listless way in which the Britishers
“knocked” about the field in the recent match with
the Juniors, meant stale as a week-oid loaf. Should
N.S.W. in the next match mind her p’s and q’s, she should
just about win.
* * *
Read and O. G. Radcliff weife put on after
other bowlers had failed to shift N.S.W. Juniors, Driver
and Pierce, and the pair of Englishmen made fair
averages with the ball.
* # #
If Percy M ‘Donnell, after two years’ absence
from the cricket-field, is considered good enough to play
against England, it seems a pity he did not come out a
few weeks earlier and show his patriotism by playing for
his province against Victoria. Rumour has it that
Percy’s resurrection is more due to the ambition of the
A.J.S. Bank than to any desire of the player.
» * *
From June 2nd to 31st December the amount
derived from the pari rnutuel (totalisator) in France for
local charities amounted to £80,0t)0. As the deduction
made from winnings for this purpose is two per cent, the
total amount invested in this mode of gambling must
have been over four millions sterling for eight months.
# # *
The invincibility of the Sheffield sluggers was
not taken for granted by level-headed journals at
“home.” The Referee, for example, in discussing the
prospects of another Australian Eleven spec, this coming
season, says “ one risk of direful financial disaster must
not be overlooked. In the event of their (Australians)
getting licked by our assortment, at present on view down
under, the bottom must be knocked out of the enter-
prise.” Such event, you will observe, was not anticipated
i n a onirit of cocksureness by a paper that is worth paying
heed to.
• * *
“ Te Whiti ” writes us repudiating the title of
“amateur champion boxer” as applied to Mr. George
Seale, of Sydney, and emphatically claiming it for Mr. Dick
Sandall, of Auckland, M.L.
# * *
Rev. Sharroek writes a droll thing in the last
English Church Magazine. He asked a parishioner’s wife
why her good man had been away from church lately.
“ Well, sir,” says she, “ he have to do a bit of betting on
Sunday, but wishes me to say his soul is with you, in the
ohurch.”
# * •
Malvolio, said to have broken down rather
seriously, is scratched for all Y.R.C. autumn engage-
ments, and the prospects of a real “picnic” for the W.
R. Wilson party look brighter every day.
* • *
When Greek meets South Australian, then
doesn’t come the tug-of-war. The latter declines to pull.
# * *
George Giffen is now quite an orator. Replying
at Adelaide welcome-back, he spoke for nearly three-
quarters of an hour. He says he always considered
Massie’s 206 against Oxford in 1882 the most brilliant
innings he ever saw, but he now thinks Lyons’ 134 against
England in Sydney eclipsed it.
« * •
The London Daily News, when the cable
reached it, giving the first innings scores of the Mel-
bourne test cricket-match, and showing England 20 runs
ahead, started out to “ skite The English cricketing
world awaits with ardor the detailed story of the triumph
of its chosen ones.” Next day was published the simple
cable—“ The Australians won by 50 runs.”
# # #
George Giffen travelled two days and nights
from Sydney after the big match, arrived in Adelaide
Saturday morning, and bagged seven wickets for 50 runs
same afternoon.
* * *
Otto Nicholson, a jockey, was killed by a fall
from a horse at Caulfield the other morning. Caulfield
course seems fatal to jocks of that name.
# * »
Bingera (N.S.W.) has had one good time in its
career, and that was when a resident secured £9000 from a
Tattersalls’ sweep. The nine thou, was knocked down in
four months, and every period of Bingera’s existence will
now be referred to as “ it was just before so-and-so won
the sweep,” or “it was just after ditto, ditto.” That
Bingera will ever experience such another time is
doubtful.
* * *

WHO'S STOLEN MY KEY HOLE?
The problem of how to find the midnight key-
hole, no matter how unsteady the hand of the
belated reveller, has been solved at last. A large,
wide-mouthed funnel, the smaller end of which is
screwed to the door, covers the otherwise frisky
and evasive keyhole. Thanks to the shape of the
inside of the funnel, the wobbling and uncertain
key is surely directed to the keyhole, which would
otherwise not be there, the weary pilgrim
finds a haven of rest —that is, if he has the right
kind of a wife, who will meet him in the hall,
gently show him upstairs, and extract his weary
feet from the reluctant boots, as well as any spare
cash which may be reposing in his breeches pocket
—and say nothing about it in the morning.
The new funnel must be regarded as a great
boon, because it has happened more than once in
society life that gentleman have been obliged to
walk the streets all night, not because they were
reporters on the morning papers, or policemen, or
even domain “dossers,” but because the keyhole
was successful in evading their keenest search.
A much greater boon, however, than the keyhole
funnel for the amelioration of family care and
worry has been before the Australian public for
the last 10 years, and the said public has not been
slow to recognise its worth. The result is, if you
ask any Australian, no matter whether he adopts
that title or is native born, what is the best
remedy for lumbago, rheumatism, sprains, or
neuralgia, he will invariably answer: “Why, St.
Jacobs Oil, of course ! ” The proof of the pudding
is in the eating, and no one who has ever used St.
Jacobs Oil as an external remedy for pain will
ever use anything else.

UP TO DATE.
The Waterbary Watches are meeting with
genuine approval on all sides. They sell upon
their merits. A discerning public has not been
slow to recognise that they are accurate, con-
venient, stylish in appearance, carefully con-
structed, and with absolutely interchangeable
parts. Their nominal price, too, is by no means
an unimportant feature. In the series for ladies
great improvements are to be noted, and these are
now turned out of a pattern and finish that cannot
fail to please the most fastidious. A word of
caution, however, is necessary. Don’t be misled
into buying inferior imitations of the Waterbury,
manufactured in Germany or Switzerland, and
not infrequently ensconced in Waterbury silk-
lined boxes the better to deceive the unwary
purchaser. Intending purchasers of the genuine
articles should note that every watch manu-
factured by the Waterbury Watch Co. has one or
both of the following distinctive marks on the
dial, viz., the word “ WATERBURY ” (in line
type) and the. interwoven letters “ W.W.C0.,”
without which no watch sold as Waterbury is
genuine.

The Late W. G. Wills.
We have all seen so many of the late W. G.
Wills’ plays that we must naturally be in-
terested a little in their author, who lately
died. Here is a picture of him —from the pen
of Augustus Moore
It will be with very sincere sorrow that poor
“Willie” Wills’ death will be heard of by all who
knew him. His work, like that of all men nowa-
days, has been absurdly overpraised, for there can
be no doubt that neither Ids painting nor his
poetry will live, for the simple reason that they
were both, judged by any high standard, about
as bad as they could possibly be. But of “Willie ”
Wills’ character nothing sweeter or more simple
in this world ever existed. I am speaking
with some knowledge, for I have known
him intimately for over 25 years. The
best part of his life was spent in one of the
Avenue studios off the Fulham Road, and never
since the world began was there a more curious
collection of artists gathered together. Next to
him lived poor young Good, the clever sculptor,
who lived with a horse and blew his brains out.
Then there was Miss Elizabeth Thompson, now
Lady Butler. But of these and other wonderful
people Wills was the most curious, and Iris studio
was the quaintest. Everybody lived there, every-
body painted there, everybody ate there and slept
there ; it was the refuge of the destitute. They
ate Wills’ food, they drank his whisky, they
wore his clothes, they pa’nted with his brushes,
they wrote with his pens, they smoked his tobacco,
and borrowed his money, and at night they covered
themselves with hiscan vases, and slept in front of his
fire. The door was never shut on anyone, and even
when Wills was writing the same went on. To
get something of silence and quiet Wills stayed in
bed all day, and wrote his curious blank verse on
little note-books about two inches square with an
infinitesimal stump of pencil. As each book was
filled he tossed it through the door to his secretary,
who somehow or other read it, and somehow or
other copied it out. This is how he did his work.
The studio was as curious as its owner and
denizens. The walls, as high as man could reach,
were one huge, chaotic memorandum book, ad-
dresses, appointments, accounts, and, indeed,
every form of memo, pictorial and literary, being
scribbled on the distemper in red, green, white,
blue, pink, black, and yellow chalks, which Wills
used for his pastelles, and nothing was funnier
than to see him trying to find out, say, when the
Princess Louise was coming to sit, and read-
ing out “Sally Hicks at 10,” “Chop, nine-
pence,” “Read play Irving,” and various
other notes extending over years. The
life, as may be imagined, was most primitive.
Wills and his secretary acted as cooks, and if you
called at lunch-time you would probably find, by
the’smell, a chop dangling from a string over the
gas-jet if the fire was not red, and often have I
seen a chop or steak, which had been sent out for,
thrown on to the fire to cook and picked out with
the tongs, and placed, black as a cinder, before an
astonished visitor. There was nothing vulgar,
inconsiderate, or pose in any or all of this curious
studio life. It was the outcome of pure simplicity
and good heartedness, for there, was nothing
sweeter than Wills’ character, for, despite his
somewhat neglige costume and habits, he was in
speech and behaviour a perfect gentleman, with
something of old-fashioned- deference which is so
charming. He had all the instincts of a poet and
a gentleman. His worst fault was his absurd
generosity. If anyone asked him for money—and
nearly everyone did—he said in his effusive Irish
way, “ Deloited, deloited,” and if he had a roll of
notes in his pocket he handed them over to his
suppliant to help himself, and if he had nothing,
as was too often the case, he would go out and
pawn his coat. He was indeed a beautiful nature.
He was absurdly good-hearted ; he meant all he
said, and he believed in his own “history,” which
was a reflection of his own ideas that kings and
gentlemen were always noble, and women were
good.
Here are some lines he wrote after the first
night of his play, “ Charles I.” :—
A FIRST NIGHT.
It comes at length the golden year,
The day that crowns my days,
My foes no longer dare to sneer,
My friends take heart to praise ;
And all is fair and all is bright
As could be wished by me—
I would that one were here to-night,
That one had lived to see.
And now I sit alone to think,
Even on a night like this,
Like some parched man who stops to drink,
Yet pauses in his bliss.
Now dreams of prose upon me break,
And nothing troubles me,
Save for this wish, this ceaseless ache,
That she had lived to see.
The rancour of old strife it dies,
Old cares I cast aside,
■ I gaze on men with kindlier eyes,
IJnwarped by want and pride,
I am aweary of the strife,
And now I will recline
In this sweet shadow of my life
This blossomed life of mine.
And all is calm, and all is bright
As could be wished by me,
And none save 1 can feel to-night,
And none save I can see.
Oh ! for her smiles that might have been,
Oli! for her lost delight,
' Her love and pride, if she had seen
The glories of to-night.
How one low word from her to me
This throned joy would crown,
The thought she did not live to see.
Strikes all my honours down.
Sept. 28, 1872.
