The Tug-of-War.
The drat night of the Tug-of-Warj at
Darlinghurst Hall, Sydney, Was a great affair;
There was a big crowd, mostly Irishmen and what
are called “foreigners.” The tugs took place on
a long narrow platform, having stout battens
nailed across it; the men laid their feet against
these battens and lay right down to their pull. It
was a straight-out test of strength and endurance;
but it was whispered about the hall that the
Irishmen and the West Indian darkies had not
room for their feet between the battens. This
should be seen to. There was a parading of teams
for a start, and a very fine-looking lot of men
they were. Then the Italians came out to pull
the Norwegians. The Italian teams were.mostly
fishermen; their rivals were sailots and wharf-
labourers, It looked any odds on the Norsemen,
who were a long way the heavier team, and they
won the pull. But the children of Garibaldi
fougnt firmly for every inch, and stuck to it for
half-an hour. It was a desperate struggle. The
crowd were very facetious, and yelled much good
advice to the fair-haired men—“ Now, boys, don : t
let the ice-cream push beat you,” “ Pull the organ-
grinders over,” “Go it, Macaroni,” —and so forth.
An interval for drinks —here the teferee took a
drink —and the Denmark team had a walk-over,
the Welshmen having scratched. The Taffies had
not time to get a team together. Then another
walk-over, France failing to appear against
Sweden. The frog-eaters rely mainly on style
and deportment in everything they do, and there
are no points given for style in tug-of-war !
# # #
Then came what was supposed to be the
tug of the evening, Australia v. Ireland. As
the teams took their places you could feel the
electricity rising in the atmosphere. The Irish
were a splendid team, a stone a man heavier than
their opponents all round, but the latter looked if
anything harder and closer-knit. As they took
their places the warning yells rang out all over the
building; “Now, boys, Sunny N.S.W. for it!”
“Go it, Australia!” And from the Irish side
came a babel of broad, soft, buttery brogue j “Git
some chark on yer hands, Dinny,” “ Mick, if yez
don’t win. niver come back to the Wharf no more,”
“For the love o’ God and my fiver, bhoysj pull
together 1 1 It was a national Irish team right
through—regular Donegal and Tipperaiy bhoys.
Ihe Swedes might have been Swedes from Surry
Hills, the Russians might have first seen the light
at Cockatoo Island, but there was no gammon
about the Irish. They were genuine; every other
ttiau answered to the name of Mike. They wore
orange and-green colours, to give the Pope and
the Protestants an equal show. Then they spat
on their enormous hands, planted tiled- brogues
against the battens, and at the stklndOf the pistol,
whiltf every Australian’s lieaft beat -high with
hope, the Mickies simply gave one enormous dray-
horse drag and fetched our countrymen clean
away hand over-hand, pulling them about ten
feet more than was necessary before they could be
stopped. How did their supporters cheer 1 Ahoo 1
Ahoo 1 The building rang again with wild shouts
of exultation. It was a great flay for Donegal
entirely, likewise for Cork and Killarney. The
Australians present palled their hats down over
their eyes and lodked at their toes. One man
wanted to back a team of light-weight jockeys to
pull anything anybody would fetch,' but' this
pleasantry could not avert the sting of defeat,
fhere was no disguising it —the Australians wore
‘ beat bad, ’ and the glory of Woolloomooloo had
departed,
* « .■*
Next came the West Indies Versus Maori-
land, which was a very funny business. The
Maprihinders Were small and weedy compared to
their sable opponents, but they hung on gamely.
The coloured gentlemen, as seen from the M.L.
end, presented a most remarkable sight. Firstly,
the eye caught the soles of their huge, fiat feet,
sticking up in the air like so many shovels; their
feet hid their bodies altogether, and only allowed
their heads to be seen-. The heads were all as
found as apples, black as ink, and each one had
in it two white dots —the glaring eyeballs of the
owner. As these remarkable people swayed in
unison behind the ramparts of their feet, they
looked like —well, it’s no good trying to say what
they looked like. There is nothing in heaven
above, or the earth beneath, or the water under
the earth, that will furnish the feeblest com-
parison. They pulled like good ; uns, and amid
loud yells of “Go it. Snowball 1” the Maorilanders
were pulled over, lighting hard to the last.
# • *
Then “Rule Britannia ” from the band,
and the English team marched on to the platform
- all very neatly dressed, neatly shaven, moving
with great precision. “What are they at all?”
“A team of marines from the men-of-war. ’ They
look'd fit to pull a house down. Then the squawk
of the band changed to “The Watch on the
Rhine, ’ and the sons of the Fatherland came up
to do or die for the country of sauer-kraut. It
looked any odds on the English. But they were
white-skinned and flaccid, while the deep, sunburnt
hueof theother arms told of hard, toughening work,
Bang went the pistol, and after a terrible tussle
the Rhinelanders fairly wore out the Britishers
and scored a gallant win. There was a strong
British section present, and their disappointment
was intense. A man-o’-war’sman was with diffi-
culty stopped from climbing on to the platform
and offering to pull the heads off the whole blanky
Hutch team. This win was a surprise, but a
bigger surprise was in store when the Russians
met the Scotch. Where they found a team of 10
Russians in Sydney goodness only knows, but
they were gaunt, wiry, hard-featured men—some
of them obviously Russian Finns, than whom the
earth produces no stronger or more resolute race.
Their opponents had a smug, comfortable look,
and seemed a long way the stouter men. The
Russians were all seamen and seafaring men of
some sort. At the signal to go, the Russians
gained a little, and then began a tremendous
wavering pull, each side alternately gaining and
losing. The Scotch supporters cheered their men
on with wild cries. The Australians impartially
barracked both sides. First they would give the
Scotch a turn—“ Go it, Donald ! ” “ Haul away,
Sandy!” “Go it, Burgoo 1 ” Then they would
turn their attention to the Russians—“ Go it,
Siberia!” “ We’ll have to get the knout to you
follows,” and so on. But all the foreign nations
seemed to form a sort of Mafia to encourage the
Russians against the Scotch. Norseman, Dane,
and Dago joined in the wild chorus of encourage-
ment. An old man, apparently the father of
one of the Russian team, danced alongside the
platform shrieking in every language under the
sun. The man he was cheering was a
great- broad-chested giant who threw his
mighty strength on to the rope in tre-
mendous surges, and at every pull the old
Whether on land or at sea, on the prairie or in
the crowded city, Ayer’s Pills are the best
cathartic, being convenient, efficacious, and sale.
For torpidity of t lie liver, indigestion, and sick
headache they never fail.
tnan would howl—“Go oil) Manuel! you’re doitig
splendid.” Then he would sing out something
like “ Kychjhnoo,” and Manuel Would give
another heave that would fetch the Scotch team
another two or three inches at least. He was a
magnificent man, was Manuel. The great wiry
muscles stood out on his arms like knotted ropes.
And when at last it only wanted site inches more
fob a win, Manltel lifted his head and gave the old
sailor-cry that the men of the sea know so well
and respond to—“ Yo, heave-ho-o-o-o-o,” and the
subjects of the Great White Czar gave one mighty
lift that fetched the Scotchmen away as if they
had been children. It was a grand pull, and
one's sympathies went with the little band
of Russians, because if they are really Russians
(their faces seettled Slavonic) there must
have been Very few men to pick from, whereas
here every third man is a Scotchman. It will be
seen, therefore, that the Germans beat the
English, the Russians beat the Scotch, and the
West India blacks beat the Maorilanders. Per-
haps (Whisper it softly)—perhaps the British and
the Australians are not the only strong and deter-
mined races on the face of the earth after all.
* * *
The Irish on the first night certainly
looked like winning. It is said that the
team has been carefully picked—-that scores of
men were tried and rejected before the team
was formed. The Continental nations have
very few men to choose from. The Australians
ought to go and practise pulling the hair off a
pound of butter before they compete. They may
do better later on. We intend to be there again
for a good long evening when the black men meet
the Irish. And if you want to get your two
eyes knocked straight into one, go and “ barrack ”
against the land of Erin.
* » •
In Australia, however, nothing is com-
plete without a strike, and on Monday the teams
mostly struck. They wanted a total sum of =£ls6
a night, and the management didn’t see it. Then
the Italian gentlemen, full of a desire to recover
their lost glory, offered to throw themselves into
the gap, and were set down as “ scabs.” By and
j bye a compromise was arrived at, and the show
began 45min. late. Scotland and Norway took
the rope and in six minutes the former went under
amid the ruins of the thistle and the haggis,
while a spectral voice in kilts groaned
over their discomfiture. Russia, with the
potent Manuel on deck, broke Germany up in
about 12 minutes, and the signs, at time of writing,
seem to be that the sons of the Great White Czar
will come out on top. Manuel —we are not
quite sure that his name is Manuel, but it
doesn’t matter —is an awful snag to strike,
especially when his aged father barracks for
him and urges him on. The longest pull of
the evening was between the Englishmen and the
West Indian darkies, and here for the first time
the Anglo-Saxon race got a show. It took them
over an hour to do it, but at the end of
that time Ham went under. He deserved
better luck, did Ham, especially as he is
the lightest team in the show. Australia again
had the distinction of knocking under in shorter
time than anybody else; he was a disgraced
kangaroo in just 33 seconds, the Maorilanders
bolting with him as if they intended to rush down
to Circular Quay. Ireland also went under to
Sweden, which was a painful surprise to many
individuals named Mick, Terence, and Hinny.
Last of all, Italy came on to give a mighty heave
for the honour of the banana-vending industry.
Denmark took the other end of the rope and held
it for 33 minutes, ahd then the stupendous efforts
of the fallen Romans carried the day. France
and Wales both failed to turn up.
* * •
After Monday night’s proceedings, Norway,
Sweden and Russia were ahead with two wins
each, and Ireland, West Indies, Italy, England
Denmark, Germany and Maoriland had one
apiece. France and Wales have been on strike
since the start, and Scotland and Australia come
in dejectedly at the tail with two blanks. If
Manuel’s boiler doesn’t burst, or his father
doesn’t break a blood - vessel while howling
for him, Russia should win the big prize ;
and Italy, if only on account of the demoniac
energy of the hulking gentleman with the
large feet who pulls at the extreme end of the
rope, should be close up. Thk BcLUStIN suggests
that that huge Roman and Manuel should pull
each other single-handed at the close of the pro-
ceedings. It would be a gaudy spectacle, The
Roman’s name, we believe, is Julius Caesar.

Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
How Babs Malone cut down the Field.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Now the squatters and the “ cockles,”
Shearers, trainers, and their jockeys
Had gathered them together for a meeting on
the flat ;
They had mustered all their forces,
Owners brought their fastest horses,
Monaro-bred—l couldn’t give them greater
praise than that.
’Twas a lovely day in Summer, .
What the blacksmith called “a hummer,”
The swelling ears of wheat and oats had lost
their tender green,
And breezes made them shiver,
M Trending westward to the river—
Ihe river of the golden sands, the moaning
Eucumbene.
If you cared to take the trouble
Y ou could watch the misty double,
ihe shadow of the flying clouds that skimmed
the Boogong’s brow,
Throwing light and shade incessant
On the Bulls’ Peaks’ ragged crescent,
Upon whose gloomy forehead lay a patch of
winter’s snow.
Idly waiting for the starting .
yl the race that he had part in,
Old Gaylad stood and champed his bit, his
weight about nine stone ;
His owner stood beside him,
Who was also going to ride him,
A shearer from Gegederick, whose name was
Ned Malone.
But Gaylad felt disgusted,
lor his joints were fairly rusted,
He longed to feel the pressure of the jockey on
his back,
And he felt that for a pin he’d
Join his mates, who loudly whinnied
or '*‘m to go and meet them at the post, upon
the track.
I roni among the waiting cattle
Game the sound of childish prattle,
And the wife brought up their babe to kiss his
father for good luck ;
' ai 'i Malone: il When lam seated
VV u * fßlylad, and am treated
ith fairish play, I’ll bet we never finish in the
ruck.”
But the babe was not contented,
Woi gU pinafore was scented
ith oranges, and sticky from his lollies, for
. he cried,
this gallant little laddie,
- s he toddled to his daddy,
id raised his arms imploringly, “Pease, dad,
div Babs a wide. .
Then the father, how he chuckled
Tli ° l • P r ide of it, and buckled
e surcingle, and placed the babe astride the
racing pad ;
He did it, though he oughtn’t,
And by Providence he shortened
The stirrups, and adjusted them to suit the
tiny lad
Who was seemingly delighted,
Not a little bit affrighted,
He sat and twined a chubby hand among the
horse’s mane,
His whip was in the other ;
But all suddenly the mother
Shrieked, “Take him off!” and then “the
field ’’came thund’ring down the plain.
’Twas the Handicap was coming,
And the rhythm of their drumming
Beat dull upon the turf that in its summer coat
was dressed,
The racehorse reared and started,
Then the flimsy bridle parted,
And Gaylad, bearing featherweight, was strid-
ing with the rest. >
That scene cannot be painted,
How the poor young mother fainted,
How the father drove his spurs into the nearest
saddle-horse,
What to do ? he had no notion,
For you’d easier turn the ocean
Than stop the Handicap that then was half-
way round the course.
On the ‘ ‘ bookies ” at their yelling,
On the cheap-jacks-at their selling,
On the crowd there fell a silence as the squadfon
passed the stand ; 1
Gayest colours flashing brightly,
And the baby clinging tightly,
A wisp of Gaylad’s mane still twisted in his,
little hand. ~
Not a thought had he of fallihg,
Though his little legs were galling,
And the wind blew out his curls behind him in
a golden stream ;
Though the motion made him dizzy,
Yet his baby-brain was busy,
For hadn’t he at length attained the substance
of his dream !
He was now a jockey really,
And he saw his duty clearly
To do his best to win and justify his father’s
pride,
8o he clicked his tongue to Gaylad,
Whisp’ring softly “ Get away, lad ; ”
The old horse cocked an ear, and put six inches
on his stride.
Then, the jockeys who were tailing
Saw a big bay horse come sailing
Through the midst of them with nothing but a
baby on his back,
And this startling apparition
Coolly took up its position
With a view of making running on the inside
of the track.
Oh, Gaylad was a beauty,
For he knew, and did his duty ;
Though his reins were flying loosely strange to
say he never fell,
But held himself together,
For his weight was but a feather ;
Bob Murphy, when he saw him, murmured
something like “Oh, hell ! ”
But Gaylad passed the filly,
Passed Jack Costigan on “ Chili,”
Cut down the coward “ Wakatip,” and chal-
lenged “ Guelder Rose
Here it was he showed his cunning,
Let the mare make all the running,
They turned into the straight at stride for
stride and nose for nose.
But Babs was just beginning
To have fears about his winning,
In fact, to tell the truth, my hero felt inclined
to cry,
For the “ Rose ” was still in blossom,
And two lengths behind her “ Possum,”
And gallant little “ Sterling,” slow but sure,
were drawing nigh.
Yes, Babsie’s heart was failing,
For he felt old Gaylad ailing ;
Another fifty yards to go, he felt his chance
was gone.
Could lie do it ? much he doubted,
Then the crowd, oh, how they shouted,
For Babs had never dropped his whip, and now
he laid it on.
Down the straight the leaders thundered
While the people cheered and wondered,
For ne’er before had any seen the equal of that
' sight,
And never will they, maybe,
See a flaxen -headed baby
Flog racehorse to the winning-post with all his
tiny might.
But Gaylad’s strength is waning,
Gone, in fact, beyond regaining,
Poor Babs is flogging hopelessly, as pale as any
ghost ;
But he looks so brave and pretty
That the “ Rose’s ” jock takes pity,
Arid, pulling back a trifle, lets the baby pass the
post.
•••. • • • •
What cheering and tinkettling
Had they after, at the “ settling,”
And how they fought to see who’d hold the
baby on his lap ;
As President Moutgom’ry,
With a brimming glass of “ Pomm’ry,”
Proposed the health of Babs Malone, who’d
won the Handicap.
BARCROFT H. BOAKE.
A scandal has been caused in Brisbane by
the pitch-forking of lecturer Brooks, ex-M.L.A.
and ex-sky-pilot, into the position of librarian at
the local School of Arts in place of Dudley
Eglington, 17 years librarian. Brooks, who wrote
a lot of fiery rant against trades-unionism, &c.,
during the shearers’strike, was a member of the
committee which found fault with and “sacked ”
Eglington, for whose dismissal no substantial
reason has been offered.
An Interesting Failure.
The dreary round of commercial failures in Mel-
bourne is brightened, now and then, by an indi-
vidual case of such exquisite humour, that the
creditors of the insolvent institution find consola-
tion in the thought that they have bought into a
first-class joke, so to speak, on fair and equitable
terms. The larger their share in the insolvency,
the more joke they get for their money. Messrs.
Tunnock and Co., Limited, a concern engaged in
the manufacture of blacking and other groceries,
recently called a meeting of creditors and share-
holders, for the purpose of considering what had
better be done under the circumstances. The
paid-up capital was some =£4ooo, the outstanding
debts amounted to another <£4ooo, and the position
of the company was simplicity itself. It had
melted into a tender memory of the past, A
demure-looking gentleman, with large, melan-
choly eyes, fired off the calm, judicial an-
nouncement that Tunnock and Co., Limited,
was as dead as the Duke of Clarence, and
its sacred ashes had become merged in the
Mercantile Loan, Finance and Guarantee Co. The
entire show was in pawn for nix, and the directors
hadn’t got the ticket. They couldn’t offer the
meeting so much as a drink out of the assets, or
suggest the dark, evil-smelling composition of a
cake of blacking in the pound. In fact, they
would have been forced to call the meeting in the
open air, and tell their side-splitting yarn upon a
piece of waste ground, but for the kind considera-
tion of the Mercantile Co. in loaning them their
lost manufactory and guaranteeing that they
wouldn’t be arrested for breaking into the
premises. The stock piled around them was no
longer theirs to offer at an alarming sacrifice.
Blacking, blacking everywhere, but not a cake to
eat. And the book-debts of the vanished com-
pany had, by some weird, mysterious, financial
arrangement, gone the same way as the boot-polish
and soap and things. All was lost save honour. The
M. L. F. and G. Co. as it appeared, administered
the “happy dispatch” to Tunnock & Co., with a
bill-of-sale, or some similar deadly instrument.
The sheriff having seized the entire bag of tricks,
put Tunnock & Co, up to auction in an unusually
quiet sort of way, whereupon the Mercantile
Finance came upon the scene, and, like a huge
boa-constrictor, swallowed the carcase of the
factory which it had already, in the ordinary way
of finance, pinched to death. The metaphors
around here are not a bit more hopelessly mixed
than the transaction in question. The price paid
by the M.L.F. & G. Co. for Tunnock & Co. at
auction was understood to be <£7o, or there-
abouts, i.e., the purchasers had bought their
own property in at that modest figure.
When the shareholders and creditors of the
annexed institution had been given time to
understand that there was nothing left to hold
shares in or prove debts against, the fun branched
off in a new direction. A committee of enquiry
recommended that the victims of this droll
calamity should form a new company for the pur-
pose of buying back the so-called “assets” of
Tunnock and Co. for .£750, from the Mercantile-
Finance crowd, who had generously consented to*
hand over the blacking, book-debts, and sundries*
for eleven times as much as they paid for them at
auction. A certain whiting mine, formerly
belonging to Tunnock and Co., was not to be
included in this proposed sale, however, probably
because it is valuable. They will take ,£750 and
keep the whiting, and the late shareholders and
creditors in Tunnock and Co. are earnestly

A distinguished Sydney equity - lawyer
now appears on the block with whiskers as black
as a crow, while his hair is as grey as a badger’s.
Not long ago a prematurely grey attorney and a
Sydney barrister, who hid his grey hairs under
cosmetics, were engaged on opposite sides in a
backblocks District Court. A question of prac-
tice arose, when the barrister sneered: “A man
at your time of life ought to know.” “You may
sneer at my grey hair if you like ; my hair,will be
grey as long as I live ; yours will be black as long
as you dye.”
One of the most guileless observations on
record appears in the report of the alleged “ Elder
exploring expedition ” :
After passing through 250 miles of desert it would have
been unwise to continue in unknown country.
Think of that! The great, expensive, gilt-edged
expedition which took its dress-suit and its eye-
glass out into the wilderness and went exploring
in a white shirt and a bell-topper grew depressed
when it saw 250 miles of unknown country, and
decided that it was “ unwise” to see any more of
it. What sort of a name would (say) Sturt have
left behind if he had collapsed every time he saw
250 miles of unknown country? The Elder ex-
pedition is the sort that ought to go exploring in
a bath-chair with a porous plaster on its chest.
A large and blusterous Yankee book-tiend
got on the razzle-dazzle the other day in aN.S. W.
bush township, and held possession of the place
for several hours. He first drank all his money
and then he drank his bogus jewellery and his
samples, and after that he drank his superfluous
clothes ; and then he was cast out of the pub. for
want of anything else to drink. Being thus
wrecked in a drought-stricken world he looked
round for someone to borrow from, and noticing
that the storekeeper was a meek-looking little
man with weeping whiskers, he walked into his
premises with a fire-eating, boiling-water-drinking
expression in his eye, and demanded the loan of
ss. Being refused, he took off his coat and jumped
on it to show that he was indignant, and explained
that if the cash wasn’t forthcoming he would
jump on the storekeeper as well, and then jump
his stock on top of the storekeeper, and the
store on top of the lot. The little, harm-
less tradesman surrendered on hearing these blood-
thirsty threats, and the book-fiend went back and
swam in liquor so long as the money lasted. When
it was gone he came back for ss. more, and got it
also. It looked, in fact, as if he had appointed
the storekeeper as his banker, and meant to draw
on him regularly for an indefinite period. But at
the fourth or fifth visit the dealer stopped the
supplies, and then the canvasser tore off his coat,
and leaped on it, and volunteered to tear the
whole shop asunder. Next he took off his hat and
dashed it on the floor, and offered to kill any man
who trod on it. It was evidently a gage of battle
and the storekeeper promptly bounded on the
hat with both feet. Then there was a long
pause. The canvasser was evidently moved, and
tears came into his eyes. Twice he tried to speak
and his voice failed him. Then in tones that were
broken by emotion he remarked : “ Old man, you
might have wiped the mud off your boots first.”
And with that he gathered up the coat and the
shattered hat and retired from the scene.

I.— A General Order.
Head Quarters, Parramatta, March 29, 18-.
A general court-martial will assemble on Thurs-
day next, the Ist April, at 10 o’clock in the fore-
noon, for trial of such prisoners as may be brought
before it.
Lieut.-Colonel Patterson, President.

ll.— Minute of the Court.
The Lieutenant-Colonel presided.
The Deputy-Judge Advocate reviewed the evi-
dence against the prisoner.
The prisoner was permitted to read a state-
ment.
The prisoner was stopped by the President, the
matter introduced into his statement being con-
sidered beyond the scope of the charge.
The court was cleared.
The court deliberated, and found prisoner,
Private John Fudgeon, 102nd Regiment, guilty of
striking his superior officer when in a state of in-
sobriety. Sentence: To be shdfto death at such
time and place as his Excellency the Lieut.-
Governor may appoint.
lII.— A General Order.
H. Qrs., 2nd April, 18-.
His Excellency the Lieutenant-Governor has
been pleased to approve and confirm the sentence
passed by a general Court martial on Private John
Fudgeon, 102iul Regiment, and hereby orders the
Regiment under Arms to attend the Execution of
the said Condemned on Monday morning next, at
10 o’clock.
Parole—Justice. Counter-Sign—Death.
IV.— The Prisoner’s Statement.
“Sir, and the Honourable Court, —I stand
charged with a crime. To understand the crime,
1 must describe my life.
“ It is now almost seven years since I received
the scrape of a pen from anyone belonging to me.
And from the following, which is an extract of a
letter I received from my eldest sister, Jeany (a
woman of very great sensibility, tenderness, and
affection, and for which I did not blame her in
the least, as she was obligated to write me in such
a manner by my father). From such an extract,
Sir, it will appear-very evident unto you that it
would be the height of folly in me to entertain
even the most distant hopes (much less sanguine
expectation's) of my connections ever doing any-
thing to forward my future welfare in life. After
a Jong preamble, resembling a Methodist parson’s
sermon, her letter concludes thus :
* A common soldier, a disgrace to your family and
connections —alas ! what have you brought yourself unto
—what money has been lavished away upon your educa-
tion and profession, all to no good purport? You might
have been a credit unto your family. You are no longer
any utility to yourself nor to society, the grand end for
which you were created. You never write to your friends,
only when you wish to drain them of money. Do you
suppose because your brother-in-law is a respectable
merchant that he is going to advance money io a man he
knows nothing at all about ? These are notions which are
very fallacious, and which will never be realised. There-
fore, as you have made your bed, so may you rest your
bones.’
“To such bitter invective, Sir—to such severe
animadversions upon my conduct in consequence
of past follies, &c. —I returned an answer as
follows :
Dear Father,—l embrace this opportunity of writing
home (if I may still, with any propriety, call it home) by
Captain W , of the N.S.W. Corps, a gentleman
whom, I informed you in my last, has, in conjunction
with my offleers in general, ever treated me with the
greatest kindness—and upon all occasions studied to ease
me to the uttermost—being stationed at an outpost on
the Hawkesbury, about 40 miles distant from Head-
Quarters.
With regard to any further particulars—l mean, with
respect to myself—l have nothing to communicate unto
you, having been repeatedly led to understand from the
tenor of your letters, as also my sister Jeany’s, that I
need look for nothing from you, nor indeed from any
of my friends, and that, as I have made my bed, so
might I rest my bones. You having, therefore, come
to such determination, it would be folly in me to the
uttermost to expatiate upon such subject—and, indeed,
to tell you the truth, it is a matter of very little indiffer-
ence to me whether you do or not, as my age and the un-
certainty of life, at the best of times, have taught me to
treat with indifference and contempt all wealth, grandeur,
&c., and to conclude with Solomon that it is all vanity,
&c. Being therefore perfectly reconciled to my fate, I
shall neither stoop so low as ’to beg, nor, indeed, to ask
any favours from any of you. Such being the case, per-
haps you may reply, what induces me to be repeatedly
writing you. My answer is that my education has taught
me it is my duty at all events to pay respect to you upon
account of your being ray parent. My letters, therefore,
to you, as also my sister Jeany, have always been couched
in the most respectful terms, but I cannot say so much
for your answers, as they have ever breathed a spirit of
the greatest rancour and malevolence, in consequence of
the follies of juvenile indiscretion, and which, from the
distance of time, ought long ere now to have been buried
in utter oblivion. But whatever errors and sallies of
youth may have precipitated me into during the former
part of my life, of this much I am very sensible, that all
of you are fully determined not to give me another trial
during the latter part of it. Such being the case, I bid
you a Anal adieu, humbly hoping that the Author of my
Existence will, whenever it shall be His pleasure to call
me hence, not deal with me quite so roughly as you have
done.
You demanded of me a certificate from my officers
respecting my military conduct. In compliance with
such your request, I herewith transmit it you—although
even here I am very sensible that such letter will avail
hut very little. You also required from me a history of
this country, which very much surprised me, upon
account of you, yourself, being an old soldier, and not
recollecting at the same time that it would neither he
political nor prudent in me, by reason of such my
situation, to enter upon such particulars.
With my natural love to you all, and compliments to
all enquiring friends.—l am, dear father, yours, &c.,
John Fudgeon.
“ This correspondence, Sir, and the Honourable
Court, will inform you of the causes of the reck-
lessness which has marked my character
The President here put it to the Court whether
prisoner should be further heard. And the Court,
deciding in the negative, the issue was as per
record.
P.W.
Joys that are No More.
Dear Bulletin,— While hunting up information cor
cerniug the early life of our dead “ Singer of the Dawn *
Henry Kendall, I stumbled across—in the Empire of’a
date 31 years ago—the enclosed little lyric, written by hi«
twin-brother, Basil E. Kendall.—Yours truly, Stewart o'"
Mitchell, Jan. 22, 1892.
See how the light recedeth, love,
The shades are falling fast,
And mantling o’er a sunless world
That weeps for glories past—
The glories of departed days
Which shone on sea and shore,
Now lost in darkness, faded out
Like joys that are no more.
How fair the moon is gleaming, love,
Through yonder shadowy trees,
Where swamp curlews their mournful songs
Chime to a mournful breeze—
The breeze that wanders here and there,
The hills and valleys o’er,
Like some lone heart, it seems to sigh
For joys that are no more.
See how the water glistens, love,
Upon the pebbly strand,
Where solemn ocean tells his tale
Of sorrow to the land.
The waves, returning constantly,
Cling fondly to the shore,
Like memory lingering round the graves
Of joys that are no more.
The morning will return, love,
And smiles will hail its birth,
And sprinkle rays of gladness o’er
A dark and cheerless earth ;
But will it bring those early days—
Those happy days of yore—
The spring-time of our life, or wake
The joys that are no more?
BASIL E. KENDALL.

The human bones found near River
have turned out to be those of an aboriginal. The
police have consequently given up the investiga-
tion. —-Newspaper Extract.
One day while digging bj' the sandy river side
“Old Bill’’found something bony—something that had
died. *
Tho’ to geology and such he’s not addicted,
Still well he knows the earth’s by nature not afflicted
With jawbones and with skullbones and a dozen ribs
Or more—he didn’t wait to count, ’em did “ his nibs.”
Man’s bones ! Foul play no doubt had placed them in
that shrine
’Midst sand and mud, where leeches crawl and wild-fowl
dine.
Plain was his duty to the law, himself and State—
The ’pleecemust get the wind, and go and seek the fate
Of one, a brother man, and run to earth the Cain—
Who had, with ruthless hand, this nameless brother slain.
The ’pleece in eager haste, like bloodhounds after prey,
Well arm’d, well mounted too, rushed madly to the
“fray”—
For don’t you kno w that clever captures mean promotion,
And don’t you know, you surely must, the great com-
motion
That dead men make, throughout this bright and sunny
land—
Those dead men that get buried somehow ’neath the sand?
The bones were rooted up and laid out in the Court,
The doctor came to view ’em and to make report.
Quoth he, “An aboriginal once held his hide
Up with these bones ;it doesn’t matter how,he died."
The ’pleece got orders to disarm. “Our luck !” they sigh.
For don’t you see, it matters not how “ darkies ” die.
Junee, N.S.W., Feb. 1, 1892.
JOHN FITZPATRICK.

Taylor and Matthias Larkin, the two
biggest embezzlers that Victoria has raised for
many years, have each a separate cell in Mel-
bourne gaol, and sundry small comforts and con-
veniences have been added to the usual furniture
in order to soothe their feelings. And meanwhile
the small thieves, who took a handkerchief or a
pair of boots apiece, are packed a little tighter
even than before to provide this accommodation,
and sleep in rows on the floor each with his foot
in the next man’s ear.
Cabled that the Czar re-institutes serfdom.
Serfdom is not an old arrangement. It was only
established about 1721, by just such a measure as
the Czar contemplates now. About half the popula-
tion were slaves, and the idea struck the nobility
of collaring the lot. So they made an Uncle
Tom’s Cabin arrangement, that the people might
be bought and sold, even families cut up—only
not by auction. The late Czar, in 1861, freed 40
million serfs—half of them Crown property, the
other 20 millions private goods.
A South African lawyer tells a story of an
ante nuptial contract. The contract is signed
before a notary by the male prior to marriage,
with a view to settling property upon the female.
An ante nuptial contract had been signed in his
presence, and the happy pair had gone on their
honeymoon and returned again, when one day
they rushed into his office with the astounding
intelligence that they had been told they
were not married. “Absurd,” said the lawyer,
“show them your certificate.” “What certifi-
cate ? ’ “ Why, the marriage-certificate given by
the minister.” “ What minister?” “The minister
who married you.” “No minister married us,”
said the wife —“you married us.” The guileless
pair had taken the anti-nuptial contract to be a
legal wedding.
The calm monotony of existence down at
Swan Hill (Vic.) was recently disturbed by the
suspicious behaviour of a lone, lorn spinster, who
first loomed upon the locality with a baby in her
arms, and was presently observed to be coming
back from somewhere in an excited state, but
without a baby. The awful tidings were spread
around by an elderly man, who drives the local
punt, and the whole township began to sniff red-
hot blood. Search parties were despatched for
the purpose of discovering the remains of the late
lamented infant wrapped in an evening newspaper,
whilst the more old and gouty section of the
population stayed behind to keep an eye on the
lorn female and see her commit self-destruction.
A first-class murder and suicide was the very
thing they wanted to wake them up at Swan Hill,
but their hopes were crushed again. The missing
baby had simply been left unexpectedly to the
care of its alleged father, and the people who went
forth to find a tiny corpse found nothing more
horrible than a flabbergasted married man wrest-
ling with the tangible result of an unhallowed
passion, and feebly requesting his injured wife to
fill the feeding-bottle. As for the spinster mother,
she put a finishing-touch to the tragedy by sud-
denly disappearing in the next train for Melbourne.
WRECKED ON THE GOODWINS.
Erasmus Darwin, grandfather of the famous
Charles Darwin, the naturalist, inscribed upon his
seal the significant words, “ Omnia ex ronchis” —
all things arise from oysters. But from what do
oysters arise? What is the origin of matter?
Ah! nobody knows. Yet one thing is getting
plainer every day. Let this man’s story show.
He goes on to say : “ On the 17th of December,
1872, whilst acting as ship’s carpenter on board
the steamship Sorrento, I was wrecked on the
Goodwin Sands. The shock to my system was so
great as to produce an effect I never felt before.
I fell into a low, weak state, and was seized with
giddiness and a sinking, fainting feeling. I had a
bad taste in the month, weight at the chest, and
great pain after eating, and my face would burn
like fire. I had difficulty in breathing and palpi-
tation, and often a pain seemed to run from the
heart through to my shoulder. I had a kind of
rumbling all over me, and a choking sensation at
the throat, with a rush of blood to the head.
“I slept very badly, and after a time I got so
nervous and. weak I was afraid to go about, and
would sit in a chair quite powerless. I saw a
doctor, who treated me for a time, and then sent
me to a hospital in Rattlebone Place, London,
whsre I was under treatment for three months,
but got no better. I then put myself under a
local doctor, who said I was suffering from
nervous debility. He patched me up for a little
while, but said to my wife, ‘Your husband is in a
critical condition, and may die at any time.
“ However, I got to work for a short time, and
then was as bad as ever. I lingered on in this
way for years—now a little better, now worse, but
never well.
“ About 1880, whilst working at Messrs. West-
wood and Bailey’s, Millwall, a mate who saw my
sufferings told me of a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and he brought me a
bottle. After taking the first bottle I found my
food agreed with me, and I began to improve.
All nervous feeling gradually left me, and by per-
severing with the medicine I was soon cured.
From that time to this I have been a strong man,
and done harder work than ever in my life before.
I have since kept Mother Seigel’s Syrup in the
house, and my wife, who was a great sufferer
from. rheumatism and dropsy, has found more
relief from it than from anything else. If any of
my family ever ail anything, a few doses soon sets
them right.”
The statement from which the foregoing is an
extract was made by Mr. William Hill, ship’s
carpenter, 108, St. Anne’s Road, London, E.
Now, what is the thing which we said is getting
plainer every day ? Why this : that almost an
infinite number of different results arise from a
single cause. Like the majority of people in all
ranks ol life, Mr. Hill had in his digestive organs
the seeds —or what women, when they buy dress
goods, call “the makings”—of dyspepsia. Up to
the winter of 1872 it may not have troubled him
much, but the excitement and exposure of the
shipwreck was more than the system could bear.
We must remember, too, that all shocks to the
mind or body, or to both, act directly on the
stomach. Who can eat when under the influence
of a great worry or a great sorrow? And if this
worry or sorrow continues long, it always upsets
digestion, permanently impairs or destroys
the appetite, and thus brings on an apparently
incurable dyspepsia and nervous debility. Why,
gracious goodness ! there are millions of women,
and lots of men, crawling about under this thing
in England every day in the year. And it is to
them we are talking. They are half dead, and
we want them to come to life, and get some good
out of their existence. Whatever the ailment is
called, you may be sure indigestion and dyspepsia
is at the bottom of it, as in Mr. Hill’s case, and
what cured him will cure you.

An of Melbourne Gas Company
has lodged a serious complaint against a lady,
who, as he alleges, is in the habit of assuming a
theatening attitude every time her gas is cut off,
an event which occurs about five times a year on
an average. Last time he went down to chop away
the meter he was entrapped into supposing that
“themissus” wasn’t at home, but directly he had
severed the service pipe and was busy plugging up
the hole, a large, muscular female started to
baste_ the back of his head. It then became a
question of whether he should let the gas escape,
or attempt to escape himself. At duty’s call he
decided to stand by the severed pipe and complete
his task. The lady of the house emptied a sauce-
pan full of Irish stew over his devoted head, and
nearly broke his arm with a dumpling, after
which she chased the unfortunate man off her
premises with a meat-axe. The Metropolitan
Gas Company is a bloated monopoly, but there
are times when the down-trodden consumer is
able to chip in and avenge a few wrongs.
The newest form of advertisement is the
professional “ window-gazer.” He is a well-
dressed individual, who walks carelessly up the
street in company with another “gazer”—they
always go in pairs. As he passes his employer's
shop he is struck all of a heap by the beauty and
cheapness of the contents, and he strikes an atti-
tude of rapt admiration before the window, and
calls the attention of his friend in a loud tone of
voice. The friend is also staggered, and they
point out the things to eacli other till a crowd
collects. Then they go on their way, and half-
an-hour later they come up the street again, and
are floored a second time. Sometimes the pair of
window-gazers work for half-a-dozen firms, and
all day long they walk round, and strike paralytic
shapes and attitudes before their bosses’ em-
poriums, A good “gazer” makes money at the
business, but it is weary work to travel about and
be an astonished citizen in front of a series of
windows for twelve hours per diem, especially
when the boss is keeping a note of his appearances,
and will dock his screw if he doesn’t turn up
twenty-four times in the day, with the same look
of surprise and admiration every time.

In the early days of the British professional-
beauty mania, when the Jersey Lily and Mrs-
Cornwallis West were struggling for supremacy,
the latter charmer was photographed in the
capacity of a romping mother, giving a x>ick-a-back
ride to a pretty little girl. That picture was all
over London, and had a big sale at a shilling.
The little girl has been growing older since then,
it appears, for she hooked a German Prince —
Henry of Pless—recently, and Albert Edward,
who used to make eyes at the lovely mother,
figured at the wedding-breakfast of her lovely
child.
For some reason which nobody has ever
explained fat men are more emotional than thin
ones. Treasurer Thomas MTlwraith (Q.), now
at war with the Bank of England, is a fair
example of this. He is a huge individual with
burning hands and hot feet and a masterful coun-
tenance, but he can be moved to tears much easier
than most men. At a banquet, when, he is the
honoured guest of the evening, and the liquor has
gone round copiously, and the room is stifling,
and there are cigar-ashes and decanters every
where, and everybody is perspiring, and the gasp*
ing congregation has opened all the windows bu
no breath of air comes in—nothing but murky
heat and green bugs and old, tired beetles—then
MTlwraith’s emotions come out strong. One
speaker after another gets up and orates about
him, and tells what a truly noble character he
is, and how much he has done for , a
suffering country, and as he listens to
them a mist of tears gathers in the big man’s
small eyes, and he makes a great effort to smother
his feelings. Then he gets up and responds with
a tremor in his voice and a look of grief on his
countenance. It is a thing to be remembered —
the spectacle of an inflamed fat man in tears. For
the fat man is apt to be bald as well as corpulent,
and when he is bald and his grief becomes too
much for him the top of his head always smokes
from perspiration and excitement and inward
combustion, and if 16 fat men were overcome at
one and the same moment they would assuredly
set the premises on fire. Why M‘IIwraith weeps
surreptitiously at these times can only be con-
jectured. Possibly he is grieved to think how
badly the orators have been taken in ; possibly he
only does it on general principles.

The fastest railway rim on record is now
the trip from New York to Buffalo (U.S.) per the
New York Central and Hudson River Railroad
The distance, 436 J miles, was done in 439innn '
and out of that eight minutes were lost in cooling
down a hot box. Stoppages were not allowed for
—the engineer had simply to keep up an average
of a mile a minute all through, and make up for
halts by extra speed. The best stretch was the
150 miles from Syracuse to East Buffalo, covered
in 140 min. lOsec. i|
